According to the lenovo website, the machine I've spec'd is a 2378RRU,
around $1700.  Anyone here have experience with one of these or
similar, plus or minus?  I've already looked at Linux on Laptops.

From creswick at gmail.com  Thu Sep  1 09:39:36 2005
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Thu, 1 Sep 2005 12:39:36 -0400
Subject: [PLUG] Thinkpad T42 comments?
In-Reply-To: <864q943iqf.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <864q943iqf.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec805090109396b2e792a@mail.gmail.com>

On 01 Sep 2005 08:34:00 -0700, Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com> wrote:
> According to the lenovo website, the machine I've spec'd is a 2378RRU,
> around $1700.  Anyone here have experience with one of these or
> similar, plus or minus?  I've already looked at Linux on Laptops.

I've been using a T42p for about a month now, and I *love* it.  (This
is the 15" screen running 1600x1200 -- my actual modes is marked "T42"
but it seems to have the hardware specs of a T42p -- which is what I
ordered).  I've had two problems with it:  1) It consumes more power
than it should while asleep in linux (not a hardware issue, as this
does not happen in windows [1], I should also add that this is still a
slow drain, it probably takes about 24 -36 hours asleep to drain the
battery), and external video is garbled at the moment (I haven't tried
to fix this yet).

I'm running Ubuntu Hoary, and the installer actually ran completely
wirelessly -- no power or ethernet, yet it was a network install :) 
in a word, it was awesome :).  Initially, I installed xandros to
resize the existing XP partition, then blew away the single xandros
partition with Hoary.  It dual boots perfectly, and I didn't have to
give it any additional thought.

Having waxed poetic about Ubuntu.. I'll move on to the hardware :)

I get a useable 4+ hours on the 9-cell battery, and there is an
ultrabay slim battery that can be added as well, although I haven't
tried this.  Hibernation works perfectly (the power issue above is
only a problem with sleep).  Aside from the increased power drain,
sleep works great.  There was a problem with the screen not being
restored on wake-up, but that is documented here [2] and the fix is
simple (kernel boot param).

The fingerprint reader is not supported, nor is the hard-drive
protection system (althought there is work on the latter).

The lcd is absolutely amazing.  Colors are very rich even at wide
viewing angles.  I often fold the screen completely flat, and everyone
around a table can see what's going on (modulo glare from direct
lighting).

A track-pad and track point are provided for pointing devices -- with
the correct configuration (I haven't done this) you can tap the track
point to left-click, and there are three mouse buttons positioned for
your thumbs, above the trackpad.  Another two buttons are located
below the trackpad, for use there.  The pad itsself is positioned and
sized perfectly *between* your hands, rather than under the heel of
your right hand. (my iBook and inspirion both suffered from crappy
trackpad placement -- drove me mad.)

You can scroll with the right side of the trackpad, as is the common
deal these days, and the pad doesn't seem to react to light/shadows,
as some crappier track pads do (an issue on my iBook and inspirion,
again).

ACPI and APM both work, and can be used in concert, I think.  (not
sure why though, ACPI does everything I need at least.)  Events are
generated for: lid button, wifi toggle, screen blank, sleep,
hibernate, and probably others.  The screen brightness controlls
appear to be in hardware, or else they "just work" too.  there is an
led on top of the lcd that shines on the keyboard without causing any
glare on the screen.

The T42 does *not* have firewire, and it only has two usb ports.  If
you're familiar with T-series thinkpads, it has all the standard
features:  swappable cd/dvd drive, (and utlrabay slim, which limits
the options, but you can still get a couple different optical drives,
a second hard drive enclosure, or another battery in there).  The main
drive pops out the side after removing one screw and tugging on the
bracket.   The dock connector is not shuttered, which I was
dissapointed by.  I've set laptops in some sticky stuff before, and
without a cover over the connector I could see that capability being
ruined by such an event.

The laptop is also *fast*.  I don't have numbers, but there are people
who do,  I just haven't had any issues with performance at all (1.8
ghz, 1.25gigs cpu scaling works great too, btw.)

Lastly, Lonovo hasn't (yet) changed the quality of their products. 
For the time being, at least, thinkpads are still (IMHO) the best
laptops out there.

Ping me if you have specific questions, and I'll do what I can to answer them :)

--Rogan

[1]:  http://thinkwiki.org/wiki/Problem_with_high_power_drain_in_ACPI_sleep

[2]: http://thinkwiki.org/wiki/Problem_with_display_remaining_black_after_resume

On Thu, 1 Sep 2005, Brent Rieck wrote:

> Vincent Yau wrote:
>> With DSL/Cable modem, they usually don't like you running services 
>> like sshd, web site etc.. that would allow incoming traffic.
>
> It's been my experience that while it may be against the TOS they 
> don't actually do anything about it unless you become a "problem". 
> To be considered a "problem" seems to involve a blindfold and a dart 
> board. YMMV by whatever ISP your DSL is through however, a few of 
> them like to monetize any incoming bits.
>
> I've been running ssh for years and haven't been told to stop by Comcast.

The Comcast Acceptable Use Policy[1] is a bit vague. You're not 
supposed to

   run programs, equipment, or servers from the Premises that provide
   network content or any other services to anyone outside of your
   Premises LAN (Local Area Network), also commonly referred to as
   public services or servers. Examples of prohibited services and
   servers include, but are not limited to, e-mail, Web hosting, file
   sharing, and proxy services and servers;

Ordinarily, I'd assume that a "Premises LAN" consists of machines, but 
the prohibition is against offering services to "anyone" (a person!) 
outside the LAN. This leads me to conclude Comcast primary considers a 
LAN a group of customers, not a physical collection of machines.

Which further leads me to conclude (or, perhaps, rationalize) that the 
prohibition isn't so much against services per se, but against 
services offered to people outside the local customer group, that is, 
folks who aren't members of your household.

So running sshd to allow you (a paying customer) remote shell access 
to your machine is probably just fine, while offering your friends 
(not paying customers) the same service is inviting trouble.

I'm sure the Comcast folks explicitly mention e-mail, Web hosting, et 
al. because those services are often made available to folks in the 
non-paying-customer category.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

[1] http://www.comcast.net/terms/use.jsp

In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509010918140.27820@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509010918140.27820@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <c2953784050901100612545c88@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/1/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>   Several years ago, Linux Journal had a step-by-step guide to identifying
> which /dev/sdaN was recognized as the inserted USB thumb drive, and how to
> mount it. I cannot find it in my collection here nor on the LJ web site.
> 
>   It's been a long time since I used that drive, and I cannot mount it. I try
> mount -t vfat /dev/sdaN /mnt/thumb
> and I'm told that the SCSI device doesn't exist.
> 
> Pointers appreciated,

The device name should show up in your log file, something like this:

Initializing USB Mass Storage driver...
scsi0 : SCSI emulation for USB Mass Storage devices
usb-storage: device found at 3
usb-storage: waiting for device to settle before scanning
usbcore: registered new driver usb-storage
USB Mass Storage support registered.
  Vendor: LEXAR     Model: JUMPDRIVE         Rev: 1000
  Type:   Direct-Access                      ANSI SCSI revision: 00
usb-storage: device scan complete
SCSI device sda: 506880 512-byte hdwr sectors (260 MB)
sda: Write Protect is off
sda: Mode Sense: 43 00 00 00
sda: assuming drive cache: write through
SCSI device sda: 506880 512-byte hdwr sectors (260 MB)
sda: Write Protect is off
sda: Mode Sense: 43 00 00 00
sda: assuming drive cache: write through
 sda: sda1
Attached scsi removable disk sda at scsi0, channel 0, id 0, lun 0

As you can see, mine showed up as /dev/sda, with only one partition, /dev/sda1.

Jason

   I found the article: "Floppies for the New Millenium", in the December 2003
issue.

   Drivers are loaded, correct entries in /proc/bus/usb/ and /proc/scsi/scsi/.
It should mount with:
                       mount -t vfat /dev/sda /mnt/thumb
but I see these messages:

mount:  bad fs type, bad option, bad superblock on /dev/sda,
         missing codepage, or other error.
         In some cases, useful info is found in syslog - try
         dmesg | tail or so.

So, I try the latter and see:

Current sd00:00: sns = 70  2
ASC=3a ASCQ= 0
Raw sense data:0x70 0x00 0x02 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x0a 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x3a 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x00 
sda : block size assumed to be 512 bytes, disk size 1GB. 
sda: test WP failed, assume Write Enabled
  /dev/scsi/host1/bus0/target0/lun0: I/O error: dev 08:00, sector 0
  I/O error: dev 08:00, sector 0
  unable to read partition table
Device not ready.  Make sure there is a disc in the drive.
Device not ready.  Make sure there is a disc in the drive.

   Does this mean the USB thumb drive has gone bad?

   What do you suggest I do?

Thanks,

Rich

In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509011003390.27820@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

Message-ID: <c2953784050901102525baffa9@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/1/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
> On Thu, 1 Sep 2005, Rich Shepard wrote:
> 
> >  Several years ago, Linux Journal had a step-by-step guide to identifying
> > which /dev/sdaN was recognized as the inserted USB thumb drive, and how to
> > mount it. I cannot find it in my collection here nor on the LJ web site.
> 
>   I found the article: "Floppies for the New Millenium", in the December 2003
> issue.
> 
>   Drivers are loaded, correct entries in /proc/bus/usb/ and /proc/scsi/scsi/.
> It should mount with:
>                       mount -t vfat /dev/sda /mnt/thumb

That should probably be "mount -t vfat /dev/sda1 /mnt/thumb", since
you want to mount the partition, not the raw device.  The message
below is probably because of that.

> but I see these messages:
> 
> mount:  bad fs type, bad option, bad superblock on /dev/sda,
>         missing codepage, or other error.
>         In some cases, useful info is found in syslog - try
>         dmesg | tail or so.
> 
> So, I try the latter and see:
> 
> Current sd00:00: sns = 70  2
> ASC=3a ASCQ= 0
> Raw sense data:0x70 0x00 0x02 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x0a 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x3a 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x00 0x00
> sda : block size assumed to be 512 bytes, disk size 1GB.
> sda: test WP failed, assume Write Enabled
>  /dev/scsi/host1/bus0/target0/lun0: I/O error: dev 08:00, sector 0
>  I/O error: dev 08:00, sector 0
>  unable to read partition table
> Device not ready.  Make sure there is a disc in the drive.
> Device not ready.  Make sure there is a disc in the drive.
> 
>   Does this mean the USB thumb drive has gone bad?

Possibly, what happens if you run "fdisk -l /dev/sda"?  Is there a
partition table?  How about if you try it on a different machine?

Flash drives are more fragile than most people think, especially when
they are formatted with FAT32, which doesn't do a good job of
distributing the writes across the memory.

Also, I read somewhere that some flash devices have slow firmware that
takes a while to initialize, and a recent patch to the kernel added a
waiting timeout.  Dunno if that got into 2.6.13 or not.

Jason

I assume that is because your partition table on the device is
corrupted, so you don't have any partitions :-(

All the flash drives I've seen come with a single partition that fills
the whole device, formatted with FAT32, since Windoze can't understand
any of the filesystems that actually belong on a flash device (like
JFFS2).

Oh, and in case you're shopping for a new one, the Lexar brand ones
are still rated the most reliable AFAIK.  Good luck!

Jason

On Thu, 1 Sep 2005, Paul Heinlein wrote:
> The Comcast Acceptable Use Policy[1] is a bit vague. You're not supposed 
> to
>
>  run programs, equipment, or servers from the Premises that provide
>  network content or any other services to anyone outside of your
>  Premises LAN (Local Area Network), also commonly referred to as
>  public services or servers. Examples of prohibited services and
>  servers include, but are not limited to, e-mail, Web hosting, file
>  sharing, and proxy services and servers;
>
> Ordinarily, I'd assume that a "Premises LAN" consists of machines, but 
> the prohibition is against offering services to "anyone" (a person!) 
> outside the LAN. This leads me to conclude Comcast primary considers a 
> LAN a group of customers, not a physical collection of machines.
>
> Which further leads me to conclude (or, perhaps, rationalize) that the 
> prohibition isn't so much against services per se, but against services 
> offered to people outside the local customer group, that is, folks who 
> aren't members of your household.
>
> So running sshd to allow you (a paying customer) remote shell access to 
> your machine is probably just fine, while offering your friends (not 
> paying customers) the same service is inviting trouble.
>
> I'm sure the Comcast folks explicitly mention e-mail, Web hosting, et 
> al. because those services are often made available to folks in the 
> non-paying-customer category.

Um, no.  The "Premises" here is the street address at which the Comcast 
link terminates.  This clearly prohibits running any services whatsoever 
that are accessible outside the Premises LAN (the network on which the 
Comcast link terminates and which is contained within the one street 
address).

So your conclusion is, in fact, mere rationalization.  This is a per se 
prohibition on services.  The choice of the word "anyone" is unfortunate, 
but not wholly inaccurate since ultimately all services are provided to 
people.

Unenforcably, this AUP also claims to prohibit running services (programs 
or equipment) that are accessible to any neighbors on the LAN if this 
extends beyond the Premises whether or not those services use the Comcast 
link.  Of course, that's based entirely on this one section of the AUP as 
I haven't read the whole thing.

J.

From ron at Opus1.COM  Thu Sep  1 12:06:49 2005
From: ron at Opus1.COM (Ron Chmara)
Date: Thu, 01 Sep 2005 12:06:49 -0700 (MST)
Subject: [PLUG] 15Mb Access
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0509011132230.19264@shaft.bitmine.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.PMDF.4.44L.0509011154040.585236602-100000@Opus1.COM>

On Thu, 1 Sep 2005, Jeme A Brelin wrote:
> On Thu, 1 Sep 2005, Paul Heinlein wrote:
> > The Comcast Acceptable Use Policy[1] is a bit vague. You're not supposed
> > to
> >
> >  run programs, equipment, or servers from the Premises that provide
> >  network content or any other services to anyone outside of your
> >  Premises LAN (Local Area Network), also commonly referred to as
> >  public services or servers. Examples of prohibited services and
> >  servers include, but are not limited to, e-mail, Web hosting, file
> >  sharing, and proxy services and servers;
> >
> > Ordinarily, I'd assume that a "Premises LAN" consists of machines, but
> > the prohibition is against offering services to "anyone" (a person!)
> > outside the LAN. This leads me to conclude Comcast primary considers a
> > LAN a group of customers, not a physical collection of machines.
> Um, no.  The "Premises" here is the street address at which the Comcast
> link terminates.

I'm not sure that's an entirely accurate parsing... the phrase 'Premises
LAN' seems to indicate that there are two components involved:
1) The Premises itself
2) The LAN found at the premises

Thus, running a file server, which a local LAN user could access while
sitting in front of their house (on a wireless link) would be allowed.
However, a longer distance pringles wireless link would probably be
considered outside of the scope of a normal LAN.

> This clearly prohibits running any services whatsoever
> that are accessible outside the Premises LAN (the network on which the
> Comcast link terminates and which is contained within the one street
> address).

I guess I would argue that the LAN is the boundary, not the physical
street address. If you have an ethernet cable that runs 10 feet onto
the sidewalk in front of your premises, they don't really care about
that.... there's no MAN or WAN links involved in such a case.

> Unenforcably, this AUP also claims to prohibit running services (programs
> or equipment) that are accessible to any neighbors on the LAN if this
> extends beyond the Premises whether or not those services use the Comcast
> link.  Of course, that's based entirely on this one section of the AUP as
> I haven't read the whole thing.

I think we're both talking about the same issue, here.... but the intent
seems to be that traffic which transverses the LAN, *and* is served to the
internet at large, is prohibited, but internal LAN servers and services
are allowed.

I suppose it comes down to how one reads "Premises LAN", and how that is
defined within the document itself, no?

-Bop

From heinlein at madboa.com  Thu Sep  1 13:33:07 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Thu, 1 Sep 2005 13:33:07 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] 15Mb Access

References: <Pine.PMDF.4.44L.0509011154040.585236602-100000@Opus1.COM>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.63.0509011316350.5252@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Thu, 1 Sep 2005, Ron Chmara wrote:

> I think we're both talking about the same issue, here.... but the 
> intent seems to be that traffic which transverses the LAN, *and* is 
> served to the internet at large, is prohibited, but internal LAN 
> servers and services are allowed.

Right, maybe.

I'd argue that Comcast's technical policies and apparent lack of 
enforcement against users providing low-bandwidth servers suggest that 
the company's working definition of "internet at large" is more geared 
toward "who" (is the remote user a customer on the premesis in 
question?) than "where" (is the machine acting as a client of that 
server physically remote from the premesis in question?).

It would be relatively easy for Comcast to block inbound SYN packets 
on popular ports to support a strict policy of "no servers."

In fact, in my neighborhood, Comcast took over what was originally the 
@Home network. The @Home admins actively scanned for rogue NNTP 
servers and would shut down those accounts if the servers persisted. 
Nowadays, I don't see any scanning like that whatsoever.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

# from Rich Shepard
# on Thursday 01 September 2005 15:15:

>?However, ... Ed Sawicki to the rescue! The drive mounts when
> addressed as /dev/sdb1. I forgot all about trying different drive
> numbers, but Ed's note to me jogged my memory and this does seem
> familiar.

Note that the drive will move around when you unplug it and/or reboot.  
Either setup a rule in /etc/udev/<something> or *always* 
tail /var/log/messages while you plug it in to make sure that you're 
trying to mount the correct drive.

I run a Suse 9.3/WinXP dual boot system, formatted with a large "D" 
drive for sharing.  To do what you want to do, move or copy the mail 
folder to the D drive. Then start thunderbird from the command line with 
the -p switch, and point your profile to the some_text.default file. 
Works for me.

On Thu, 1 Sep 2005, Russell Senior wrote:

> Paul> It would be relatively easy for Comcast to block inbound SYN 
> Paul> packets on popular ports to support a strict policy of "no 
> Paul> servers."
>
> But bear in mind, they could start being draconian at any time. 
> And since it is their official policy, you'd have no recourse 
> (except to drop them like a loaf of moldy bread, of course).

That is so true. Until recently, Qwest said my phone line didn't 
qualify for DSL. I don't know why it now qualifies, but I've been 
starting to investigate the option. I'd much rather send my ISP fees 
to a local firm like Spire or EasyStreet.

The trouble is, the US Supreme Court's FCC v. Brand X decision has 
seemingly given the FCC (and the Bush administration) the green light 
to deregulate DSL and remove line-sharing requirements.

My fear is that once Qwest is no longer legally bound to offer access 
to third-party ISPs, DSL will become as monolithic as cable. At this 
point, it's only a fear, but it's very tough to overestimate a 
monopoly's irrational drive for short-term profit.

  -- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0509012028440.10664@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20050902035541.GZ32742@bavariati.org>

On Thu, Sep 01, 2005 at 08:30:58PM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Thu, 1 Sep 2005, Aaron Burt wrote:
> 
> >Couple things:
> >1. Can you even ping the host's network interfaces, and/or anything
> >  on the network outside the host?
> 
> Aaron,
> 
>   Haven't tried. Don't know where to do this in the virtual win98.

>From the command line.  Start/Programs/Command Line

You should be able to use "ipconfig" to see if you have a working
network interface, and "ping" to see if you can contact the guest's
IP address, the host's IP address and something outside the host, like
google.com.

>From the host, you should also be able to ping the guest's IP address.

On 9/1/05, Rich Shepard wrote:

>    Er, no. It's past the 30 days and they won't talk to me. Sales helped by
> sending me the proper serial number and I've posted on the vmware user forum
> for workstation support.
> 
>    VMware is a great product, but their support model is that of Redmond and
> does not win them any friends. I'd no longer recommend their product just
> because they want to suck their clients dry for support.

I'll second this.  One of the worst support experiences of my life was
with VMware.  After several frustrating days trying to work out a
crash bug I was having with VMware, I tried to get the completely
clueless support drone to escalate to his manager.  He wouldn't.  He
offered to transfer me to another clueless support drone, but under no
circumstances could I have the problem escalated.

I finally had to write a note to the sales folks telling them what a
horrific time I'd had-- they managed to track down a really sharp guy
who almost immediately found the problem.  (It was me doing something
stupid.  Oops.)

I've recently been talking with VMware support again and they seem to
have cleaned up their act significantly.

My guess is they danced around a bit with offshoring and came back
when it was costing them business.

  -- Steve

PS: Rich, I found a possible solution and posted it to the VMware
board.  It applies to 4.5.2 but may also work for 5.0.  Basically,
turn off encrypted passwords in the Samba you'll be using, whether
VMware-provided or your own.


From jeme at brelin.net  Thu Sep  1 21:27:39 2005

Subject: [PLUG] DVD+-WTF
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0509012110560.19264@shaft.bitmine.net>


OK, I'm having data throughput problems.  I don't think I should, but 
maybe somebody knows better.  The motherboard is some Tyan Tiger model 
(don't recall which at the moment).  Dual Athlon MP 1.8GHz (whatever 
number designation that is).  1GB RAM.  No swap.

/dev/hdb -- 120GB IDE drive
jbrelin at person:~/school$ hdparm /dev/hdb

/dev/hdb:
  multcount    =  0 (off)
  IO_support   =  1 (32-bit)
  unmaskirq    =  1 (on)
  using_dma    =  1 (on)
  keepsettings =  0 (off)
  readonly     =  0 (off)
  readahead    = 256 (on)
  geometry     = 65535/16/63, sectors = 240121728, start = 0


/dev/hdc -- DVD+-RW drive
jbrelin at person:~/school$ hdparm /dev/hdc

/dev/hdc:
  IO_support   =  0 (default 16-bit)
  unmaskirq    =  0 (off)
  using_dma    =  1 (on)
  keepsettings =  0 (off)
  readonly     =  0 (off)
  readahead    = 256 (on)
  HDIO_GETGEO failed: Invalid argument

/dev/hdd -- DVD-ROM
/dev/hdd:
  IO_support   =  1 (32-bit)
  unmaskirq    =  1 (on)
  using_dma    =  1 (on)
  keepsettings =  0 (off)
  readonly     =  0 (off)
  readahead    = 256 (on)
  HDIO_GETGEO failed: Invalid argument

/dev/sda -- 20 GB SCSI HD


OK, so I'm trying to write some DVDs.

Created an ISO in a fairly normal way (may as well be a big pile of 
zeroes, though).  Using growisofs to burn with
growisofs -Z /dev/hdc=file.iso

It chugs along for a while (varies wildly) and then the write speed drops 
to 0.0 for a while and the failure pops up.

Seems to me like some buffer is emptying, but you'd think if I shut 
everything else down and ran the thing from tty1, it'd do better.  It 
doesn't.

The few times it's appeared to work, the disks had some files that 
consistently could not be read from any drive (though most of the data can 
be read from any drive I tried).

What the hell am I missing?

J.

From cstevens at gencom.us  Thu Sep  1 21:54:16 2005
From: cstevens at gencom.us (D. Cooper Stevenson)
Date: Thu, 01 Sep 2005 21:54:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DVD+-WTF
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0509012110560.19264@shaft.bitmine.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0509012110560.19264@shaft.bitmine.net>
Message-ID: <1125636856.12465.4.camel@localhost>

My wife had this trouble just yesterday--she fixed it (she's my 'secret
weapon).

I'll forward this on to her... she'll be able to provide you with the
details. I'm sketchy on the details, but I know that she burned the
DVD's firmware. 

The key, I think, was modifying the "growisofs" command.

It's worthy of note that we found out a lot about squirelly DVD
manufacturers and the tricks the pull. DVD owners in the U.S, for
example--and again I'm sketchy on the details--should change their DVD
firmware from "Region 1" to "Region 2." The difference, as I recall, is
whether or not you have the ability to burn non-signed files or
something to that effect.

More on this as it becomes available.


-Coop

I will be travelling for a month and a half, criss-crossing the northern
hemisphere, to bring the world of Ubuntu to your doorstep. Expect
badgers,
mushrooms, schnaaaaaakes, an awful lot of Ubuntu CDs, and a healthy dose
of
madcap hijinks! If you would like to catch up with me during my journey,
add
your details to the wiki page, or reply to this email.

Where will I be?

 * Boston for the GNOME Summit
 * London to check out our office
 * Amsterdam for EuroOSCON
 * Milan for Smau e-Academy
 * Montreal for UbuntuBelowZero
 * (US tour surprise - see below!)
 * Mexico City for GULEV

So... What's the surprise? Well, I've left a big gap open in my
itinerary
for you to fill in! It's kinda like reality TV, but without the TV, and
with
a lot more airline food. Here's how it works:

Would you like me to visit your local LUG or Ubuntu LoCo Team? If you
live
in a US city with a major airport, and can arrange a venue and audience
on
one of these dates, you have the chance to rearrange my itinerary in
your
favour!

All you have to do is create a virtual petition of twenty supporters -
the
more creative the better. You could get twenty of your user group
members to
post their support on a mailing list thread... You could get twenty
friends
to blog about it... You could put up a photo of your LoCoTeam in a huge
Ubuntu circle... Or you could come up with something even cooler!

 * Send your entry to me, Jeff Waugh <jeff.waugh at ubuntu.com>

 * Submissions close September 30th. The first ones in will be the
easiest
   to award - get in quick!

 * Please include a link to your virtual petition, along with your
contact
   details (phone number, in particular)

Good luck, Ubunteros!

From seniorr at aracnet.com  Sat Sep  3 01:56:16 2005
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 03 Sep 2005 01:56:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] combination bootable Knoppix & FAT partition on USB Flash
	Drive
Message-ID: <864q92wmvj.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


I'd like to make a bootable custom Knoppix (or Knoppix-like) system on
a USB Flash Drive (e.g. a 512mb or 1gig SanDisk Cruzer Mini) that also
contains a windows- and Knoppix-mountable FAT partition that Knoppix
(and windows) can write to.  I am looking for a minimal-footprint
Linux environment in which to run my data collection software, and
that allows collected files to be saved to stable media without
risking touching any precious hard disk data on their machines.

I've been googling around and found some pieces.  Anyone have
experience putting Knoppix on a USB Flash Drive or have any other good
pointers?

Thanks!


-- 
Russell Senior         ``I have nine fingers; you have ten.''
seniorr at aracnet.com


From athlonrob at axpr.net  Sat Sep  3 08:39:33 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Sat, 03 Sep 2005 08:39:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] combination bootable Knoppix & FAT partition on USB Flash
	Drive
In-Reply-To: <864q92wmvj.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <864q92wmvj.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <4319C3B5.10409@axpr.net>

Russell Senior wrote:
> I've been googling around and found some pieces.  Anyone have
> experience putting Knoppix on a USB Flash Drive or have any other good
> pointers?

It's not that hard.  Back in December or so I put the SECURE (?) 
knoppix-like mini-CD distro on my 512MB USB stick.  It's just files 
sitting in a FAT32 filesystem.  As long as the kernel can read the 
filesystem the compressed data image sits on, you're good to go - be it 
iso9660 or fat32.  That part of the equation was relatively simple.

The more difficult tasks were getting a working bootloader and modifying 
the boot scripts to look for a USB stick instead of just a CD.  I fought 
and fought to get syslinux or isolinux to work on a USB stick (one of 
them advertised that as being possible at the time) but eventually gave 
up and used GRUB instead.  Google should be able to help you configure 
GRUB properly to work - it didn't install properly for me, forcing me to 
install it again from within the grub prompt booted off the USB stick.

The boot scripts in the little initrd-style filesystem need to be 
modified to load the USB storage drivers and to then check the proper 
/dev/sd? locations instead of checking the CD-ROM drive for the 
"knoppix" image.

I don't think I had to do anything else to get it working.....

Rob

>Can't locate Date/Calc.pm in @INC (@INC contains:
>/usr/lib/perl5/5.8.6/i486-linux /usr/lib/perl5/5.8.6

Did you maybe upgrade perl?  If so, your @INC might have changed.  Try a 

  find /usr/ -name 'Calc.pm'

It's also possible that your package manager decided that you didn't 
need it anymore (given that your program is in /usr/local/bin/, I'm 
guessing that it wouldn't know about the dependency.)  Or, you may have 
a corrupt disk.

From ckonstan at lunarlogic.com  Sat Sep  3 12:46:00 2005
From: ckonstan at lunarlogic.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 3 Sep 2005 12:46:00 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Running Browser From Cron?
In-Reply-To: <4318F0C4.2050406@comcast.net>
References: <4318F0C4.2050406@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0509031156130.6482@localhost>

Xvfb might be the answer.  We use it to run our megabloated tomcat app
on remote machines.  The app requires X resources for some reason.

The poor man's way for us to run the app on a remote machine is to run
it in a vnc session.  This allows the ability to disconnect from the
server without it being the wiser, since the X session remains alive and
well.  While our app can be started and run in an ssh shell, it cannot
be backgrounded, and the screen session in which we get the ssh shell to
the server cannot be detached, without the server dying.  I'm no expert
on this stuff (I'm not a sysadmin, I'm a code drone), but I do know we
solve the problem with Xvfb.  It looks like another way to keep an X
display session alive so an X-dependent app can have have an environment
to run in.

Since our servers do not run any X session other then the Xvfb one, the
sysadmins are at liberty to hardcode :0 into their scripts.  This does
not work on a workstation, where the user is likely to be running :0
already, and possibly others in the form of multiple graphical X
sessions and vnc sessions.  You need some dynamic way to find the next
available display.  If I find out how, I'll add a posting later.  I
think our scripts were actually fixed to remove the hardcodings.  I can
take a look.

Carlos

> display session alive so an X-dependent app can have have an environment
> to run in.
> 
> Since our servers do not run any X session other then the Xvfb one, the
> sysadmins are at liberty to hardcode :0 into their scripts.  This does
> not work on a workstation, where the user is likely to be running :0
> already, and possibly others in the form of multiple graphical X
> sessions and vnc sessions.  You need some dynamic way to find the next
> available display.  If I find out how, I'll add a posting later.  I
> think our scripts were actually fixed to remove the hardcodings.  I can
> take a look.
> 
> Carlos
> 
> On Fri, 2 Sep 2005, Robert McIntosh wrote:
> 
> > Date: Fri, 02 Sep 2005 17:39:32 -0700
> > From: Robert McIntosh <mcintoshrt at gmail.com>
> > Reply-To: "General Linux/UNIX discussion and help; civil and on-topic"
> >     <plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> > To: plug at lists.pdxlinux.org
> > Subject: [PLUG] Running Browser From Cron?
> > 
> > I'm trying to get a cron job to load a browser page as a user multiple
> > times and then close.  Those of you with devious minds see what I'm
> > getting at.
> >
> > I would use lynx or wget, but I need a browser with javascript to load
> > the page.  Odd request, I know.
> >
> > I'm working with:
> >
> > #!/bin/sh
> > /usr/X11R6/bin/mozilla --display :0 http://www.somewebsite.com
> > exit
> >
> > and cron whines at me:
> >
> > (mozilla-bin:2725): Gtk-WARNING **: cannot open display: :0
> >
> > Please provide hints or blatant help.
> >
> > Thanks!
> > Robert
> >
> > -- 
> > Robert McIntosh
> > mcintoshrt at Comcast.net

Since you are not logging into the system from a terminal, you do not
have permission to display on :0.  You will need to allow localhost to
use the display.  Use the xhost +localhost command to provide that
permission on the session the "owns" the display.

Ken Stephens
CAD 2 CAM

Subject: [PLUG] SAMBA [NL] Win 95 adm WinXP
In-Reply-To: <431A03B1.6020009@acm.org>

Message-ID: <1125779637.17419.3.camel@atlas.cadtocam.com>

On Sat, 2005-09-03 at 13:12 -0700, Roderick A. Anderson wrote: 
> Not strickly Linux so ignore as you see fit.
> 
> I just installed a new WinXP system ( for a company that would love to 
> give up Windows but has applications with no alternatives ) with several 
> _very_ legacy Win9x systems using SAMBA file shares and printers.
> 
> To get the WinXP system to use the shares I had to turn on encrypted 
> passwords in SAMBA and now am having problems with the Win9x system 
> getting to the printers.  The system keeps asking for a password.
> 
> This ring a bell for anyone?  Pointers, clue-stick, etc.
> 
> 
> Rod

Look in /usr/share/doc/samba-3.0.10/registry directory for the proper
register entry to allow encrypted passwords on the Win9x.  This is
directory listing is from a FC3 distribution.  You version of samba and
distro may put it somewhere else.  Look for the registry hacks, though.

Ken Stephens
CAD 2 CAM

> I just installed a new WinXP system ( for a company that would love to
> give up Windows but has applications with no alternatives ) with several
> _very_ legacy Win9x systems using SAMBA file shares and printers.
>
> To get the WinXP system to use the shares I had to turn on encrypted
> passwords in SAMBA and now am having problems with the Win9x system
> getting to the printers.  The system keeps asking for a password.
>
> This ring a bell for anyone?  Pointers, clue-stick, etc.
>

You have to create user accounts on Win9x. Then windows automatically sends in 
the username of whoever is logged in, so all the user has to do is enter 
their Samba password.

I believe Windows caches the Samba password, "helpfully" relieving users of 
the tedium of typing it in every time. Which is such an obvious security hole 
I'm sure I don't need to go into the gory details. If I remember correctly, 
that is, it's been awhile since I adminned win9x on Samba domains. (sometimes 
the brain blocks out awful memories.)

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Mon Sep  5 07:31:22 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Mon, 5 Sep 2005 07:31:22 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Broken Inbound Mail System
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509050722510.4439@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   I have apparently done something bad to my mail system, and I cannot figure
out what I did despite trying for the past two days. I've had no mail in my
INBOX since Friday. No spam that slipped past the filters, no reports on
backup results or the pflogsumm report from /var/log/maillog, no duplicates
sent in response to mail list messages. Mail from various mail lists are
placed in the proper files by procmail, but nothing comes into the inbox.

   This all started, I think, when I tried to modify procmail recipes to
direct moderately high volume mail lists into the appropriate file rather
than /var/spool/mail/rshepard. Nothing from those mail lists has shown up
since Friday, either. I've tried changing the order of recipes, but something
broke, either inadvertently by me or for some other reason. I need to find
the source of this problem and fix it, and I need help doing so.

   I asked on the mailhelp list about the lack of MTA log analysis, and the
sudden inability for /usr/local/bin/pflogsumm to run, but I've not received
help to a solution there.

   How do I go about trouble shooting this problem?

TIA,

Rich

Dr. Richard B. Shepard, President     |   Author of "Quantifying Environmental

From srau at rauhaus.org  Mon Sep  5 08:04:48 2005
From: srau at rauhaus.org (Stafford A. Rau)
Date: Mon, 5 Sep 2005 08:04:48 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Broken Inbound Mail System
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509050722510.4439@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509050722510.4439@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20050905150448.GB385@rauhaus.org>

* Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> [050905 07:31]:
> 
>   How do I go about trouble shooting this problem?

Do you have something similar to the following at the beginning of your
.procmailrc file?

MAILDIR=$HOME/Mail
LOGFILE=$MAILDIR/log

That will create a log file in your $MAILDIR directory that indicates
what procmail is doing with incoming messages.

Also, do you have something like this:

DEFAULT=$MAILDIR/inbox

That will dump anything that isn't matched by your recipies into the
mail file by that name.

--Stafford

Subject: [PLUG] Broken Inbound Mail System -- FIXED.
In-Reply-To: <20050905150448.GB385@rauhaus.org>

From johnxj at comcast.net  Mon Sep  5 22:50:34 2005
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jordan)
Date: Mon, 5 Sep 2005 22:50:34 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] That's It! Nautilus is HISTORY!
Message-ID: <431CCBBA.13175.A64516F6@localhost>

I just lost about 15 Gb of files. Permanently gone. This happened 
while trying to burn them to a DVD for backup. Yes, there is no 
backup.

Originally I selected the whole folder (about 20 Gb.). But Nautilus 
does not tell you how many bytes the folder is unless you select 
all the files. Not knowing how many there were, I dragged them all 
to a burn window. When I tried to burn a DVD it did not tell me that 
there were too many files to fit. No, the stupid thing said there was 
no DVD in the drive. What it was trying to tell me was that there 
was not a DVD capable of holding 20 Gb in the drive. But the only 
error message it knows is "No disk in drive."

So after removing and replacing the blank DVD various times I 
decided to use a CDR instead, and this time I knew it couldn't hold 
all the files, so I selected just 600 Mb of them in the burn window. 
But once again, I got the error message. Turns out that whatever is 
in the burn window is what it wants to burn. Never mind that you 
have selected only some of them.

OK, have to remove the others from the burn window. But from past 
experience, this is very dangerous. You see, it did not occur to the 
Nautilus programmers that it would be useful to show different 
icons for file vs. shortcut to file. In other words, there is no way to 
tell whether you are deleting the file, or a shortcut to it. 

After thinking about this for a bit I decided I'd drag the extra files 
back to the original folder. This, I reasoned, would generate an error 
message if what was in the burn window was really a copy of the 
file instead of just a shortcut to it.

And it did, indeed, generate the error message. Fine. "I'll just 
cancel this operation then," I thought. Except the programmers 
forgot to put a cancel button on the error message box. So I tried 
hitting the X in the corner of the box, but it was grayed out. So 
were the Xs in the windows for the burn window and the original 
folder window. And the only buttons on the error message window 
were Skip, Overwrite, and Overwrite All. So I clicked on Skip. And 
it skipped the file. But there were 301 files. Looked like I'd have to 
click on Skip 301 times. They also forgot to put in a button for Skip 
All. So I decided, "if these are just copies of the files, let it go 
ahead and overwrite them -- won't make any difference." So I 
clicked on Overwrite All. And guess what effing Nautilus proceeded 
to do? It overwrote 301 files with NADA. They all disappeared. 
They're not in the Trash, they are nowhere. Gone. Just plain gone. 

This is a Very Bad Bug. 

I have to manipulate files constantly on this computer and the 
command line is just too time consuming. Seldom do I need to 
move files with something in common so I can use wildcards. 
Usually I want just "this file" and "that file" and "that other file" and 
so on. Too much typing to do it from the command line. I really 
depend on a GUI file browser.

Tonight's exercise has be so furious that if I can't get a decent file 
browser that actually works reliably and has a user interface that 
has actually been thought out, I swear I'm gonna unsubscribe, wipe 
out Linux, and install Windows 2000. I just lost four days work. I've 
HAD IT.

And save the pontificating. "You should have been making backups 
as you went along" is not going to make me feel any better about 
an OS whose users think it's OK to have a file browser that 
crashes several times a day and destroys files.

On Tue, 6 Sep 2005, Rogan Creswick wrote:

> You have my sympathies as well.  I have no love for Nautilus, for many reasons.

   John can add my support, too.

> While I personally like the command line, I've head that the Rox Filer is
> very cool.

   While I _much_ prefer the command line, there are a few GUI applications
that have become indespensible. One of the most powerful aspects of linux
over anything Microsoft offers is choice. Sometimes it seems that there's too
much choice.

   IMO, both KDE and Gnome try to replicate the look and feel of Windous. This
is, for me, a total turn-off. There are so many alternatives to those two
that it can sometimes be difficult to choose.

   I've been using Xfce for years: lightweight, fast, does everything I need.
But, that's only my preference and YMMV. There are abundant tools, too, for
working with groups of files at one time. Take a couple of deep breaths,
John, and look at them. There's plenty of willing help here, but anger and
frustration are reasonable responses when something breaks Big Time and
you're not aware of what to do about it.

   As an aside, I had that sinking feeling this morning when I tried to
upgrade the postfix package here. It was only after upgrading -- and seeing
all the error messages -- that I discovered that the default download
requires mysql. I don't need or want mysql; my mail system needs no database
backend at all. So, several minutes of reading messages in the package forum
left last July showed me where to go to get cyrus-sasl and postfix packages
built without mysql support because others had asked for that, too.
Obviously, I'm back to having a working mail system -- upgraded to 2.2.5.
But, how do you ask for help from the community when the mail isn't working?
_That's_ a conundrum.

   Your file problem can almost certainly be fixed. Let's take it one step at
a time.

Rich

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Tue Sep  6 07:55:37 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Tue, 6 Sep 2005 07:55:37 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Why does this procmail recipe fail to work properly?
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509060752090.22363@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   A mail list to which I am subscribed sends messages that have this line in
the header:

To: matplotlib-users at lists.sourceforge.net

   One of my procmail recipes is:

:0:
* ^TO_: .*matplotlib-users at lists.sourceforge.net
programming

   Yet all incoming messages end up in my inbox rather than the 'programming'
file.

   Is there an obvious error I don't see?

TIA,

From travislspencer at gmail.com  Tue Sep  6 08:02:38 2005
From: travislspencer at gmail.com (Travis Spencer)
Date: Tue, 6 Sep 2005 08:02:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Why does this procmail recipe fail to work properly?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509060752090.22363@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509060752090.22363@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <e3b7bac05090608026c3e35c4@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/6/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
> :0:
> * ^TO_: .*matplotlib-users at lists.sourceforge.net
> programming

When using the special, uber regex `^TO_', don't use the colon,
period, and splat:

:0:
* ^TO_matplotlib-users at lists.sourceforge.net
programming

HTH.

-- 

Regards,

Travis Spencer

On 9/5/05, John Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> I just lost about 15 Gb of files. Permanently gone. This happened
> while trying to burn them to a DVD for backup. Yes, there is no
> backup.
> 
> Originally I selected the whole folder (about 20 Gb.). But Nautilus
> does not tell you how many bytes the folder is unless you select
> all the files. Not knowing how many there were, I dragged them all
> to a burn window. When I tried to burn a DVD it did not tell me that
> there were too many files to fit. No, the stupid thing said there was
> no DVD in the drive. What it was trying to tell me was that there
> was not a DVD capable of holding 20 Gb in the drive. But the only
> error message it knows is "No disk in drive."
> 
> So after removing and replacing the blank DVD various times I
> decided to use a CDR instead, and this time I knew it couldn't hold
> all the files, so I selected just 600 Mb of them in the burn window.
> But once again, I got the error message. Turns out that whatever is
> in the burn window is what it wants to burn. Never mind that you
> have selected only some of them.
> 
> OK, have to remove the others from the burn window. But from past
> experience, this is very dangerous. You see, it did not occur to the
> Nautilus programmers that it would be useful to show different
> icons for file vs. shortcut to file. In other words, there is no way to
> tell whether you are deleting the file, or a shortcut to it.
> 
> After thinking about this for a bit I decided I'd drag the extra files
> back to the original folder. This, I reasoned, would generate an error
> message if what was in the burn window was really a copy of the
> file instead of just a shortcut to it.
> 
> And it did, indeed, generate the error message. Fine. "I'll just
> cancel this operation then," I thought. Except the programmers
> forgot to put a cancel button on the error message box. So I tried
> hitting the X in the corner of the box, but it was grayed out. So
> were the Xs in the windows for the burn window and the original
> folder window. And the only buttons on the error message window
> were Skip, Overwrite, and Overwrite All. So I clicked on Skip. And
> it skipped the file. But there were 301 files. Looked like I'd have to
> click on Skip 301 times. They also forgot to put in a button for Skip
> All. So I decided, "if these are just copies of the files, let it go
> ahead and overwrite them -- won't make any difference." So I
> clicked on Overwrite All. And guess what effing Nautilus proceeded
> to do? It overwrote 301 files with NADA. They all disappeared.
> They're not in the Trash, they are nowhere. Gone. Just plain gone.
> 
> This is a Very Bad Bug.
> 
> I have to manipulate files constantly on this computer and the
> command line is just too time consuming. Seldom do I need to
> move files with something in common so I can use wildcards.
> Usually I want just "this file" and "that file" and "that other file" and
> so on. Too much typing to do it from the command line. I really
> depend on a GUI file browser.
> 
> Tonight's exercise has be so furious that if I can't get a decent file
> browser that actually works reliably and has a user interface that
> has actually been thought out, I swear I'm gonna unsubscribe, wipe
> out Linux, and install Windows 2000. I just lost four days work. I've
> HAD IT.
> 
> And save the pontificating. "You should have been making backups
> as you went along" is not going to make me feel any better about
> an OS whose users think it's OK to have a file browser that
> crashes several times a day and destroys files.

Wow, I feel for you man.  I hope you can get your data back, you
probably want to look into one of the computer forensics toolkits. 
Try not to do anything with the partition your data was on until you
can get the files back, if possible, the less you do with it the more
likely you'll be able to "undelete" them.

I would REALLY like to encourage you to (when you're feeling calm, but
not too calm) take your story to Ubuntu, or perhaps the Nautilus guys
directly.  This is the kind of stuff they really need (and want!) to
hear.  File a bug, spam a mailing list, whatever, but make sure
someone hears about this (besides PLUG :-), and you might be surprised
by what you find in the next version of Nautilus.

Jason

On 24 August 2005 at 12:12, Kevin Cosgrove <kevinc at doink.com> wrote:

> I'm having a heck of a time getting my SCSI devices (2 hard
> drives, a DAT tape drive, and a CD-RW drive) to be accessible
> on MDK10.1 and MDK10.2.  The two versions are 1 each on two
> different machines.  On one machine I'm using a BusLogic SCSI
> card.  On the other I'm using an Adaptec SCSI card.  The BIOS
> of both machines recognizes the cards and the attached devices.
> Linux on both machines recognizes the SCSI cards, but neither
> machine will show any attached SCSI devices.  I used to run
> MDK10.0 on the machine that is now MDK10.1, and the SCSI hard
> drives worked just fine at that time.  I was running a 2.6 kernel
> on MDK10.0, and I'm running 2.6 kernels on both machines now.

I managed to get my SCSI devices to be detected on Mandriva
LE2005.  The trick was th reinstall.  The BusLogic card was not
in the system the first time I installed.  After a reinstall
everything on the SCSI card's buss is detected.  This is on
my Athlon, ABIT system.  Next I'll try my Celeron, Intel mobo
system.

FYI....

From fpikus at gmail.com  Tue Sep  6 11:01:50 2005
From: fpikus at gmail.com (Fedor Pikus)
Date: Tue, 6 Sep 2005 11:01:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Nforce vs. VIA for Linux?
In-Reply-To: <20050906142341.59801.qmail@web52611.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20050906142341.59801.qmail@web52611.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <a4fbdc7b050906110132b0546e@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/6/05, Scott Van Hoosen <svanhoosen at yahoo.com> wrote:
> 
> A couple years ago, I got a motherboard with an Nforce chipset, but was
> unable to get the onboard NIC to work for lack of driver support. Since
> then I have stuck with motherboards with VIA chipsets.
> 
> I'm wondering if things have improved with Nforce on Linux. What has
> been everyones' experience recently with Nforce?


NVidia releases a binary driver for their Ethernet chip. There is also an 
open-source driver, forcedeth, but it did not work for me.

NVidia also includes OSS driver for their audio chips, but ALSA supports 
them as well. It's just a regular ALC850 chip. 

NVidia IDE is supported by the regular IDE driver, and NVidia SATA is 
supported by sata_nv which is included in 2.6 kernels and newer 2.4 kernels.

Some vendors add more hardware to their boards, my Asus board has a second 
NIC (Marvell chip, supported by sk98lin) and a second SATA controller 
(Silicon Magic, sata_sil). 

-- 
Fedor G Pikus (fpikus at gmail.com)
http://www.pikus.net
http://wild-light.com

On 9/6/05, Scott Van Hoosen <svanhoosen at yahoo.com> wrote:
> A couple years ago, I got a motherboard with an Nforce chipset, but was
> unable to get the onboard NIC to work for lack of driver support. Since
> then I have stuck with motherboards with VIA chipsets.
> 
> I'm wondering if things have improved with Nforce on Linux. What has
> been everyones' experience recently with Nforce?

My laptop has some variant of an Nforce3 chipset on it, but it uses
the Realtek 10/100 NIC (8139too driver).  As usual, the Broadcom
wireless only works with ndiswrapper.

Also, it is my understanding that portions of the Nforce4 chipset
definitely do not work, and will never work, under Linux, such as the
SATA drive with NCQ support.

Jason

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Tue Sep  6 13:15:23 2005

In-Reply-To: <BAY108-F351780E94E711011AEC7E5B8A70@phx.gbl>
References: <BAY108-F351780E94E711011AEC7E5B8A70@phx.gbl>
Message-ID: <c29537840509061315554e4ecc@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/6/05, Vincent Yau <v_yau3 at hotmail.com> wrote:
> Hi:
> 
> I am on kernel 2.4.21-32 and wish to upgrade to 2.6.12 or 13.  I did a
> kernel config
> and compile the kernel.  Upon reboot, I picked 2.6.12,
> it loaded several things and finally gave me a bunch of error messages:
> 
>   insmod exit abnormally
>   insmod: QM_MODULES: Function not implemented.
> 
> Could anyone tell me how I can fix this error?
> thanks

You need to get module-init-tools, as the module loading/unloading
tools changed between 2.4 kernels and 2.6 kernels.  You should be able
to install the new version and it will work backwards compatible with
your 2.4 kernel, if you need to keep it around.

Jason

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Tue Sep  6 17:25:36 2005
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Tue, 06 Sep 2005 17:25:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Counting Paragraphs in a Text File
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509061659490.17659@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509061659490.17659@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <1126052736.26634.31.camel@eel.myhealthbank.com>

On Tue, 2005-09-06 at 17:01 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>    'wc' will tell me the number of characters, words, and lines in a text
> file. Is there a simple utility that will tell me how many paragraphs there
> are if each paragraph is defined by two newlines?

Sure; you can change the record separator in Awk and count the records:

awk -v RS='' 'END { print "Paragraphs:", FNR }' filename.txt

(See the man page for RS='' instead of RS='\n\n'.)

AIX's grep has a paragraph mode, which is useful not only for text but
also finding relevant stanzas in configuration files and utility output.
With the above technique, you can achieve the same result.

Remember, of course, that "awk '/FOO/'" is basically the same as 'grep
FOO' (the record-print is implicit without another action). So you can
apply the same trick:

awk -v RS='' '/FOO/'

and find paragraphs with "FOO".  Unfortunately, the paragraphs
themselves are broken with this, so you can add them back in with:

awk -v RS='' '/FOO/ {print $0, "\n"}'

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd. <http://nakedape.cc>

From nickaubert at gmail.com  Tue Sep  6 18:44:13 2005

Date: Tue, 6 Sep 2005 18:44:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] That's It! Nautilus is HISTORY!
In-Reply-To: <200509061630.39300.alex@daniloff.com>
References: <431CCBBA.13175.A64516F6@localhost>
	<200509061630.39300.alex@daniloff.com>
Message-ID: <55ef240d050906184422124e10@mail.gmail.com>

Rich Shepard mentioned 'mc' which also has an 'undelete' feature you
might be interested in checking out.

A few years ago, I ran into some bug in gqview which deleted about
half my hard drive until I killed the process.  I wasn't ever able to
reproduce the error (in a controlled environment, obviously), so I
don't know what went wrong.  Anyway, I shut off the computer and
mounted the hard drive on a different machine and used mc's undelete
function to recover almost all of the files that had been lost.  This
was on an ext3 filesystem.  I don't know what filesystem you're using;
if you don't know either, it's probably ext3.

Anyway, before you do anything else to your hard drive (like
reformatting it and installing Windows), you might want to have a look
at 'mc' to see if you can recover your data.  To be safe, I would take
out the drive and mount it from another host before installing any new
software on it.

-Nick

Scott Van Hoosen wrote:
> A couple years ago, I got a motherboard with an Nforce chipset, but was
> unable to get the onboard NIC to work for lack of driver support. Since
> then I have stuck with motherboards with VIA chipsets.
> 
> I'm wondering if things have improved with Nforce on Linux. What has
> been everyones' experience recently with Nforce?

I *love* my nForce-based systems.  If you don't mind using a recent
(2.6.8+ I'd say) kernel, everything should work OOTB.  Forcedeth has
worked quite well for me.  Audio works fine with the i8x0 driver.  IDE
works great.  Integrated video works as long as I set it in the BIOS to
use as much of the RAM as I can (setting it to 16MB causes the nVidia
drivers to not work properly - then again, this was a few years ago...)

In-Reply-To: <1126052736.26634.31.camel@eel.myhealthbank.com>

On 9/6/05, Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc> wrote:
> On Tue, 2005-09-06 at 17:01 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
> >    'wc' will tell me the number of characters, words, and lines in a text
> > file. Is there a simple utility that will tell me how many paragraphs there
> > are if each paragraph is defined by two newlines?
> 
> Sure; you can change the record separator in Awk and count the records:
> 
> awk -v RS='' 'END { print "Paragraphs:", FNR }' filename.txt

If a paragraph is *exactly* two new line characters in a row, then a
less accurate way that is a little easier on the wrists would be to
just use grep like this: `grep -c ^$'.  For instance:

> man procmailrc | awk -v RS='' 'END { print "Paragraphs:", FNR }'
Paragraphs: 149
> echo -n "Paragraphs: "; man procmailrc | grep -c ^$
Paragraphs: 153

-- 

Regards,

Travis Spencer

In-Reply-To: <431E51BF.5030008@axpr.net>

	<200509061630.39300.alex@daniloff.com> <431E51BF.5030008@axpr.net>

On Tue, 6 Sep 2005, AthlonRob wrote:

> Hang in there... we've all done it (deleted a bunch of files accidentally)
> before and have lived to tell about it!

> Perhaps we should all share our "root oops" stories or something to make
> the new guy feel better?  :-)

   OK. I'll start.

   One day, soon after I received my new Sony notebook from Emperor Linux, I
was doing some administrative work -- su'd to root -- and meant to type 'rm
-f usr/'. However, for reasons I need not mention, I was seriously distracted
and typed 'rm -f /usr'. Of course, I instantly recognized my error, but there
was nothing to do but wait until the entire filesystem was deleted. Sigh.

   Fortunately, the fine folks at Emperor Linux know that we mere mortals get
distracted and do foolish things by accident. They have a recovery partition
on the hard drive. One telephone call and a half-hour later and all was well
again.

   Then there was the time when I -- as a user -- meant to type 'rm -f .*~',
but the fickle finger of fate was too fast for my slow brain and I hit the
[Enter] key before the tilde. Do you know just how many dot-files you have in
_your_ home directory? Can you name them?

   In this case, a trip to the back office brought forth the last full backup
and a restore of all ~/.* fixed that up.

   These two instances are why I 1) backup every night (incrementally) and do
a full backup each Friday night and 2) always make an alias for rm so that it
asks me if I really want to do the deletion. Sometimes, being a touch typist
has its down side.

Rich

Ctrl-C is your friend.  Or even the power cord, if necessary.  

Any invocation of "rm -rf" includes two non-printing characters:
<pause> and <think>, before the <enter> key.

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Sep  7 06:34:10 2005
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 7 Sep 2005 09:34:10 -0400
Subject: [PLUG] That's It! Nautilus is HISTORY!

	<200509061630.39300.alex@daniloff.com> <431E51BF.5030008@axpr.net>

	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0509070618010.15061@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80509070634230de75@mail.gmail.com>

I was once changing distributions from (Mandrake 6?) to some version
of slack, and in advance I had jotted some notes about my partition
table on a napkin, knowing that I would need this when I went to
format the drive. (I wanted to save /home, as well as another rather
large partition full of music.)  Since I was revamping the rest of my
partition table, I only noted the partitions I wanted to save:
/dev/hdc1 and /dev/hdc6. (note they were both on hdC).  Having written
that down, I instantly forgot it.

When it came time to partition with fdisk (I didn't know about cfdisk,
or perhaps it wasn't available.. anyhow, I didn't have an easy view of
the partition table / sizes while I was partitioning) I looked at my
napkin, and saw "Oh, home is /dev/hda1 and media is /dev/hda6".  as
the ink had bled a bit, and my hurried handwritting made the c's
connect, and obviously /dev/hdo wasn't an option.

The install finished, and I logged in to find that /home contained the
root file structure from a Mandrake 6 install, and /media was really
just my old /usr.   Close to 30 gigs toast (this was a good 5-6 years
ago), and all my current projects, cvs repositories, homework, and
hours on hours worth of configuration data.

I *almost* quit using linux, but eventually realized what had gone wrong.

--Rogan

From travislspencer at gmail.com  Wed Sep  7 10:11:36 2005
From: travislspencer at gmail.com (Travis Spencer)
Date: Wed, 7 Sep 2005 10:11:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Counting Paragraphs in a Text File
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509070614230.15061@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

	<1126052736.26634.31.camel@eel.myhealthbank.com>
	<e3b7bac05090620195dd577af@mail.gmail.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0509070614230.15061@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <e3b7bac0509071011475b2d2b@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/7/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    Thanks, Travis. I don't understand the difference in the resulting counts,

The difference is that Wil's solution will correctly handle situations
where the number of blank lines between paragraphs is greater than
one.  Also, my quick and dirty solution counts the number of blanks
between paragraphs.  The actual number of paragraphs (even if only one
new line separates them) is one greater than the number of lines
separating them.  For example:

> echo -n "Paragraphs: " ; cat <<EOF | grep -c ^$ 
Test para

Test para 2

Test para 3


Test para 4


Test para 5

Test para 6
EOF
Paragraphs: 7
> cat <<EOF | awk -v RS='' 'END { print "Paragraphs:", FNR }'
Test para

Test para 2

Test para 3


Test para 4


Test para 5

Test para 6
EOF
Paragraphs: 6
> echo -n "Paragraphs: " ; cat <<EOF | grep -c ^$ Test para

Test para 2

Test para 3

Test para 4

Test para 5

Test para 6
EOF
Paragraphs: 5
> cat <<EOF | awk -v RS='' 'END { print "Paragraphs:", FN R }'
Test para

Test para 2

Test para 3

Test para 4

Test para 5

Test para 6
EOF
Paragraphs: 6

> but my immediate need is for a correct number. 

Ya, then Wil hooked ya up.  His solution is a good one.

-- 

Regards,

Travis Spencer

On Tue, Sep 06, 2005 at 07:34:39PM -0700, AthlonRob wrote:
> Perhaps we should all share our "root oops" stories or something to make
> the new guy feel better?  :-)

Back in 2001, I was given the wonderful opportunity to maintain 
a Legato backup infrastructure.

The main Solaris filer (about 1TB) was backed up to a Legato 
Networker server (Linux) with a 16 tape Jukebox attached.  
There were also about a dozen other Linux and HPUX boxes backed 
up to this Networker server.

At one point I needed to do a full restore of a Linux box.  I 
loaded the restore program and began a restore.

About 3/4 the way through I remembered that Legato used the 
model where you run the restore program _on the box you want to 
restore_ as it uses a pull model, not push.

Well.. now I had just overwritten the entire backup server with 
this one server's data, including the Networker files related 
to the backup system.  Poof!  There goes the entire database of 
data to tapes tables.  Now where did I leave that backup system 
to the backup system?

27 hours later, I was back to normal with no data or tape 
library loss.  I learned a lot about the inner workings of 
Legato that day.

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

From ron at braithwaites.net  Thu Sep  8 09:26:08 2005
From: ron at braithwaites.net (Ron Braithwaite)
Date: Thu, 8 Sep 2005 09:26:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] That's It! Nautilus is HISTORY!
In-Reply-To: <431E51BF.5030008@axpr.net>
References: <431CCBBA.13175.A64516F6@localhost>
	<200509061630.39300.alex@daniloff.com> <431E51BF.5030008@axpr.net>
Message-ID: <E4E34283-611D-4137-9596-212B05B328C4@braithwaites.net>

On Sep 6, 2005, at 7:34 PM, AthlonRob wrote:
> Perhaps we should all share our "root oops" stories or something to  
> make
> the new guy feel better?  :-)

Well, a long time ago I had just taken a job as a systems programmer  
in beautiful San Bernardino, armpit of the universe (I was living in  
Idyllwild and commuting). A large part of the job involved writing  
microcode for the Data General MV20000, which led to a number of very  
funny stories (not necessarily funny at the time, though). I won't go  
into the reasons why you had to use microcode to get any significant  
systems level work done, but suffice it to say that the MV series had  
4 special purpose 32 bit registers available to the compilers and  
macro assembler, while there were 16 general purpose 32 bit registers  
if you used microcode.

At any rate, I was writing a test suite to bang on the MUMPS compiler/ 
interpreter that I was working on and I was told by my manager not to  
mess with the development machine, since they were doing something  
there as well. So I log into the production machine to run my  
innocuous little test.

Except it wasn't that innocuous.

When the test routine to check the date function, which had to  
support both Gregorian and Julian dates, I just slammed the hardware  
timedate back to a couple thousand years ago and marched it forward.

Very shortly afterwards, there was a mob outside my office door with  
pitchforks and torches and tar and feathers. Seriously, there were  
probably over 20 people in the hall yelling for my head on a platter.

It seems that we had essentially replace MV/OS with our own  
customized MUMPS operating system (very funny stories around MUMPS,  
believe you me), which used the timedate function to stamp the  
virtual memory pages. When I whacked back timedate a couple thousand  
years, there were about 60 people trying to actually do real work on  
the machine. Many of them had not saved their work for *days* and it  
was all gone.

I no longer do any work on production machines, even if management  
tells me to. I can laugh at it now, but there were several people in  
danger of aneurisms and I think I suffered some hearing loss that day.

Peace,
-Ron

Ron Braithwaite
2015 NE 37th Ave
Portland, OR 97212 USA
503-267-3250
ron at braithwaites.net

On Tue, 06 Sep 2005, Jason wrote: 
[...]
> I've had to rebuild my /home folder a couple of times because I wanted
> to remove a lot of .doc files.  So I did 'rm * .doc' instead of 'rm
> *.doc'.  Doh!

BTDT :-\  Now I protect dirs I care about by creating a `-i' file so
that wildcard expansion by rm sees it as an option. To wit...

[sandy at pickled ~]$ mkdir rmtest
[sandy at pickled ~]$ cd rmtest
[sandy at pickled rmtest]$ touch this that .the .other thing
[sandy at pickled rmtest]$ touch -- -i
[sandy at pickled rmtest]$ ls -a
.  ..  -i  .other  that  .the  thing  this
[sandy at pickled rmtest]$ rm -rf t *
rm: cannot lstat `t': No such file or directory
rm: remove regular empty file `that'? n
rm: remove regular empty file `thing'? n
rm: remove regular empty file `this'? n

Of course, that still won't protect dot files...

[sandy at pickled rmtest]$ rm -rf .t*
[sandy at pickled rmtest]$ ls -a
.  ..  -i  .other  that  thing  this

Sandy

backpanel
    |
    |
    |
    |	 --------------------------------
    |  	|      	       	       	  |    	 |  5V slot
    |	 --------------------------------
    |				  ^ 5V key
    |
    |
    |
    |
    |
    |	 --------------------------------
    |  	|      |       	       	       	 |  3.3V slot
    |	 --------------------------------
    |	       ^ 3.3V key
    |


The ASUS P2B has 4 PCI slots.  All are 5V slots.

figure I moved about 40 tons manually, every bit of it myself, some 
more than once. I came home every night with every bone in my 
body aching. Clearly I was cut out for a sedentary occupation.

Now, getting back to the issue, the first thing I did after discovering 
the problem was to log out/shut down the computer (a laptop). It 
hasn't been turned back on since. So if there is any way to recover 
the files, they should still be on the hard disk.

Some have suggested Midnight Commander for this. I can see if 
Ubuntu-64 shows it in Synaptic. Lots of programs do not appear 
because they won't run in a 64-bit distro. Installing it, however, will 
require disk writes, which may corrupt what's left of the files. Still, 
something's better than nothing, so I'll give it a shot this weekend.

Someone else suggested Rox Filer as an alternative for Nautilus. 
I'll try it out after I exhaust all possibilities of recovering the lost 
files. In the meantime, any additional suggestions for a drag and 
drop file browser are welcome. I don't care about pretty -- I just care 
that it _works_. And, of course, it has to work on 64-bit Ubuntu. It 
would take a lot to get me to abandon Ubuntu.

An additional matter is that I need to figure out how to do backups 
with rsync. Someone gave me a couple lines to use, but they don't 
work. The following is my network situation and what I need to do:

Linux (Ubuntu-64) laptop
Windows 2000 desktop
Several large laser printers (not relevant in this matter)

There is a 200 Gb external USB drive connected to the Windows 
computer. I created two partitions, one NTFS for backups of the 
Windows computer, and the other FAT32 for backups from the 
Linux computer. The FAT32 partition is shared and the Linux latop 
has no problem reading/writing to it.

The line I was given was "rsync -a /path/to/be/backed/up 
/path/to/backup/to." When I try this line (substituting my path 
information), it just gives me error messages. I think the problem is 
that I have not specified the path with correct syntax. For the 
record, the FAT32 partition I created for the Linux backups is seen 
by Windows as f:\. Can someone tell me exactly what to type at 
the command line to create a backup to f:\ from the command line? 
And once having this successfully created, is there a way to do the 
update periodically, like one half hour after turning the laptop on 
(because right after turning it on I probably want to use the full 
power of the CPU and hard disk, not have to share them with 
rsync).

Thanks again for everyone's suggestions and commiseration. I feel 
better now, although physically exhausted. :)

On Wednesday 07 September 2005 04:30 am, Eric Wilhelm wrote:
> # from AthlonRob
>
> # on Tuesday 06 September 2005 21:34:
> >Perhaps we should all share our "root oops" stories or something to
> > make the new guy feel better? ?:-)
>
> My funniest (and most educational) would definitely be when I was
> sitting at my wife's computer and trying to remove a file with a funny
> filename (maybe it started with '-'.)  I had typed "rm -rf" and had hit
> the backspace key before I remembered that she had one of those evil
> keyboards with the backwards L-shaped enter key!  Now I always put the
> -rf options to rm at the end after I type the filenames, but more
> importantly I have banned all evil keyboards.
>
> --Eric
Hello!
One day I decided it was time to organize the files in my /home directory. I 
got the idea that the best command to use was rm -Rdf ./*  to delete the 
contents of directories I no longer wanted.

Everything was going fine. Files were being put where they needed to be (using 
other commands of course, then I just started to delete things I had no use 
for. I typed the command mentioned above and then all of a sudden I noticed 
the directory I was in was /home. I quickly hit the ctrl-c then ctrl-x hoping 
one would stop the process before everything was gone. The process stopped. I 
was really glad that I had a very large set of files starting with B. I only 
lost about a gig and most of it was not needed. I no longer do this, though 
it works quite well!

We all do dumb stuff, especially when we know better!

Dale


From jtodd at loligo.com  Fri Sep  9 09:52:15 2005
From: jtodd at loligo.com (John Todd)
Date: Fri, 9 Sep 2005 09:52:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Need corporate volunteer for Asterisk testing
Message-ID: <p0602048bbf476bd8ba59@loligo.com>


I'm developing a configuration suite for Asterisk using the Soekris 
4801 boxes and "astlinux", which is a compact-flash loaded version of 
Linux for those devices.  The 4801 has a PRI/T1 connection on the 
back of the unit.  I hope to have some "how-to" documents for 
small/medium businesses and educational institutions, but I need to 
have a real, live PRI system to test with (my own experiments with 
back-to-back Asterisk servers just don't cut it - I need an actual 
PBX to test with.)  I have a talk to give at Astercon in October on 
this topic, and I'd like to have some actual tested configs to hand 
out.

I would like to see if any small (or medium, or whatever) business 
would allow me to "experiment" with your PBX during off hours, with 
your assistance.  My goal is to get your PBX routing all it's calls 
through a variety of VoIP methods if the destination of the call is 
reachable via VoIP, and if not, to have the call flow normally 
through your existing system.

1) You need to have at least 10 people using  your phone switch

2) Your phone switch must be connected to the PSTN via a PRI (sorry, 
no FXO lines or key systems)

3) You must have a free PRI port on your switch that can make/take 
calls as if it was a PSTN trunk

4) You must be willing/able to change routing on the phone switch 
during experiment periods.  This does not need to be during 
"production" hours.  You should have a decent understanding of how 
your PBX works.

5) You must be willing/able to have an Internet connection going to 
my laptop and the Soekris during the test intervals.

6) Greater Portland area is fine.  Weekends or after hours is fine; 
preferred, in fact.  I'll come over to your site.


I can offer nothing in exchange, other than a brain dump of what I'm 
doing and how you could do it yourself.  If you don't have a VoIP 
system, I'm probably someone you would be interested in talking with 
(for free!) since I have done integrations like this for several 
years, and I would be happy to offer free advice or discussions while 
I tinker with getting this particular configuration working.

Please contact jtodd at loligo.com for details!  Thanks in advance.

JT

Volunteer Database for Technology Help at Evacuee Shelters 

PublicWebStations.com has been helping to provide Internet access in
disaster relief shelters.

We have received many request from volunteers for a way to make their
availability known. This new website, TechVolunteers.com, is where you
can register yourself as a volunteer, or can find a volunteer who is
local to you. The links to register or locate volunteers are to the
right hand site on the top of the front page.

We also encourage you to continue to use and check the discussion
forum for PublicWebStations.com, for project coordination and where
other opportunities to help may be listed. The link to the discussion
forum is: http://www.desktoplinux.com/cgi-bin/board/UltraBoard.pl?Action=ShowBoard&Board=katrina

If you don't find an easier way, take a look at the Sleuth Kit at
http://www.sleuthkit.org.  I would recommend using something like the
Penguin Sleuth Bootable CD from http://www.linux-forensics.com,
instead of using your installed distro.  In particular try some of the
stuff mentioned here:

http://www.sleuthkit.org/sleuthkit/docs/ref_fs.html

This will likely be only feasible for specific files you want to
recover, since I imagine it would take forever otherwise.

Jason

# from Rich Shepard
# on Saturday 10 September 2005 12:03:

>Are these systems Microsoft-specific?

I imagine not.

>I had no light on the card so 
>'/sbin/iwconfig eth1' did nothing of value. I had no idea what else to
> try.

This should show you all of the AP's that your card can see (and info 
about what kind of setup they use.)

  iwlist eth1 scan

Of course, make sure that your card didn't move from eth1 to wlan0 or 
whatever (a bare iwconfig will verify this.)  Also, you may need to use 
ifup (or ifconfig) to get the card configured with dhcp, etc.

From travislspencer at gmail.com  Sat Sep 10 12:33:19 2005
From: travislspencer at gmail.com (Travis Spencer)
Date: Sat, 10 Sep 2005 12:33:19 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Question about bash scripting -- specifically `set -u'
Message-ID: <e3b7bac05091012334594987f@mail.gmail.com>

Hey All,

I want to play it safe and tell bash to disallow the use of unbound
variables by calling `set -u' at the top of my script.  This is fine
until I check to see if the user has provided the script's required
arguments.  If they haven't, I get an `unbound variable' error.  For
instance, take this little script:

#! /bin/bash

set -u

if [ -n "$1" ]; then
    echo "OK.  \$1 = $1"
else
    echo "You must provide one argument."
    echo "Usage: ${0##*/} ARG"
    exit 1
fi

If the user provides the one required argument `$1' is bound and the
condition works fine; however, if the user invokes the script without
the required argument, the check for `$1' will cause an error such as
`argtest.sh: line 5: $1: unbound variable'.

When using the nounset option, how are you supposed to check for
arguments that may not have been provided?

TIA!

-- 

Regards,

Travis Spencer
