From travislspencer at gmail.com  Sat Sep 10 13:40:22 2005
From: travislspencer at gmail.com (Travis Spencer)
Date: Sat, 10 Sep 2005 13:40:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Question about bash scripting -- specifically `set -u'
In-Reply-To: <e3b7bac05091012334594987f@mail.gmail.com>
References: <e3b7bac05091012334594987f@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <e3b7bac050910134015a6d636@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/10/05, Travis Spencer <travislspencer at gmail.com> wrote:
> This is fine
> until I check to see if the user has provided the script's required
> arguments.  If they haven't, I get an `unbound variable' error.  

Figured it out.

> set -u
> 
> if [ -n "$1" ]; then
>     echo "OK.  \$1 = $1"
> else
>     echo "You must provide one argument."
>     echo "Usage: ${0##*/} ARG"
>     exit 1
> fi

set -u

if [ ${1:-0} ]; then
    echo "You must provide one argument."
    echo "Usage: ${0##*/} ARG"
    exit 1
else
    echo "OK. \$1 = $1"
fi

> TIA!

All thanks go to me I guess :-)

-- 

Regards,

Travis Spencer

On Sun, 11 Sep 2005, Vincent Yau wrote:

> I am looking into what are all the different disk backup options in Linux.

Vincent,

   I've been using BRU for eight years now without any complaints whatsoever.
They changed hands several years ago (the employees bought the company), and
changed their pricing policies, but I suspect they're still in business
despite not advertising in LJ every month.

   I stopped upgrading -- and paying for unneeded support -- several years
ago. The application has worked flawlessly for me, daily/weekly backups and
restores of single files, directories, or the whole system onto a new hard
drive.

   While it has an X interface, I never used that. It runs as a cron job
Monday through Friday nights and just flat out does the job it's supposed to
do. If they're still in business I recommend them without reservation.

Rich

etc. should be "Sender ok") and have it delivered to user `sandy'. But I
don't want to allow dictionary attacks (e.g. red at herring.org should be
"User unknown"). I've got over 100 addresses of the form sandy_<foo> for
which receipt needs to happen. Any time I've had to supply an address to
a web vendor, I've used sandy_vendorDomain at herring.org so that I can
track address misuse.

Even an explicit virtusertable entry such as...

sandy_test at herring.org	sandy

...isn't working. The separator above /is/ a <tab>. And I did do...

makemap hash /etc/mail/virtusertable < /etc/mail/virtusertable

...after creating the entry. Here's the dialog...

[bigfish at onyx|~] $ telnet herring.org 25
Trying 216.99.203.246...
Connected to herring.org.
Escape character is '^]'.
220 pickled.herring ESMTP Sendmail 8.13.1/8.13.1; Sun, 11 Sep 2005 07:31:13 -0700
helo aracnet.com
250 pickled.herring Hello onyx.spiritone.com [216.99.193.114], pleased to meet you
mail from: bigfish at aracnet.com
250 2.1.0 bigfish at aracnet.com... Sender ok
rcpt to: sandy_test
550 5.1.1 sandy_test... User unknown
rcpt to: sandy
250 2.1.5 sandy... Recipient ok


Ideally, I'd like to have working...

sandy_+ at herring.org sandy

...which I did try. After that failed, I reasoned I should see if even a
simple case would work. Here's the relevant bits from my sendmail.mc...

define(`confPRIVACY_FLAGS', `authwarnings,novrfy,noexpn,restrictqrun')dnl
FEATURE(`virtusertable',`hash -o /etc/mail/virtusertable.db')dnl
FEATURE(redirect)dnl
FEATURE(always_add_domain)dnl
LOCAL_DOMAIN(`localhost.localdomain')dnl
FEATURE(`preserve_local_plus_detail')

I'm obviously not understanding how to use virtusertable, much less
wildcards - any /sendmail/ help would be appreciated.

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Sun Sep 11 10:00:13 2005
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Sun, 11 Sep 2005 11:00:13 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Sed problems
In-Reply-To: <1126446685.8513.7.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <1126446685.8513.7.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0509111000519cae46@mail.gmail.com>

That always works for me... off hand I'd guess that your file isn't in
UNIX format, though I believe /^$/ would match CRLF or NL.  'file
FILENAME' will tell you if it is in DOS format (output will be:  ASCII
text, with CRLF line terminators) Try running dos2unix on your source
file if that is the case.

/eli

/etc/aliases. I was hoping I could use a wildcard to handle it, but have
since learned that magic of that sort requires hacking sendmail.cf
directly. I'm loathe to do that. It's a pity that few web sites allow
entering a plus-address'd email - that would have handled it neatly. Too
many squeeze out the plus sign *sigh*.

From dwight.hubbard at gmail.com  Mon Sep 12 09:17:09 2005
From: dwight.hubbard at gmail.com (Dwight Hubbard)
Date: Mon, 12 Sep 2005 09:17:09 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linux backup option
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509102001230.9952@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <BAY108-F17D4E18B2065ABE7B7F294B89A0@phx.gbl>
	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0509102001230.9952@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <321871a605091209172d4cabd2@mail.gmail.com>

What are you looking to back up?

Do you want to be able to backup the filesystem or the database or both?

If you want to back up the database, what methods would you consider 
adequate:
export and backup export files as part of a filesystem backup

put the database in archive log mode and do a backup and take it back out of 
archive log mode when finished

have extra disk to mirror the database to which you put into archive log 
mode and break the mirror and then backup the mirror

hotbackup using EBU or RMAN

If you want to backup the database and just backups of the exports aren't 
adequate it will cost you some money either for extra disk to use for 
mirroring/archive logs or the purchase of a commercial backup client like 
Legato Networker, Symantec Netbackup or Arkeia with the oracle backup 
modules. In some cases you might want to do both.

I personally have backed up oracle hot using RMAN or both Legato Networker 
and Netbackup, they both work well, but neither is cheap.

Message-ID: <20050912095450.521b7059f1e09bbdacd7e3b34a5d9af3.a2f000b21b.wbe@email.email.secureserver.net>

If you're looking for a great Linux-based backup that can do other OS's
as well as your databases in hot mode, check out CA's ARCserve for
Linux.  It rocks.

Dave

> -------- Original Message --------
> Subject: Re: [PLUG] Linux backup option
> From: Dwight Hubbard <dwight.hubbard at gmail.com>
> Date: Mon, September 12, 2005 12:17 pm
> To: "General Linux/UNIX discussion and help, civil and on-topic"
> <plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> 
> What are you looking to back up?
> 
> Do you want to be able to backup the filesystem or the database or both?
> 
> If you want to back up the database, what methods would you consider 
> adequate:
> export and backup export files as part of a filesystem backup
> 
> put the database in archive log mode and do a backup and take it back out of 
> archive log mode when finished
> 
> have extra disk to mirror the database to which you put into archive log 
> mode and break the mirror and then backup the mirror
> 
> hotbackup using EBU or RMAN
> 
> If you want to backup the database and just backups of the exports aren't 
> adequate it will cost you some money either for extra disk to use for 
> mirroring/archive logs or the purchase of a commercial backup client like 
> Legato Networker, Symantec Netbackup or Arkeia with the oracle backup 
> modules. In some cases you might want to do both.
> 
> I personally have backed up oracle hot using RMAN or both Legato Networker 
> and Netbackup, they both work well, but neither is cheap.
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

From plug at martinconsulting.com  Mon Sep 12 11:06:46 2005
From: plug at martinconsulting.com (Brian Martin)
Date: Mon, 12 Sep 2005 11:06:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] RE: Re: 15Mb Access
In-Reply-To: <20050831222049.B8AC7406E@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <20050912180650.3EBCC13674@mail.silverflash.net>

OK, so I got went on vacation before getting a chance to reply to everyone
on my 15Mb access problem and solution.  Below is an omnibus reply to a
variety of responses.  I've edited down the replies for easy readability.

> From: Charlie Schluting 
> 
> Nothing.. you're not using Gigabit..
> 
> But how are you testing? Don't use those bandwidth speed sites.. they 
> aren't even well enough connected to test 6Mb/s cable..
> 
> I'm curious how fast you can download from here: 
> http://unix.schluting.com
> 

I'm not sure what you mean by "you're not using Gigabit".  I'm trying to get
15Mb through an adapter that's capable of 100Mb, so I don't see a limitation
there.

But you're right that the bandwidth speed sites aren't adequate for testing.
I downloaded Knoppix from your site.  It took about 6.1 minutes to download
about 700MB.  If I've done my math right I'm getting 15.3Mb/second.

    700MB * 8 = 5600Mb
    6.1 minutes * 60 = 366 seconds
    5600/366 = 15.3 Mb/second

That's rough, because I haven't figured in the TCP/IP overhead and perhaps
other factors.  Still, I think it tells me my firewall is doing fine.  Did I
make any gross mistakes?

> From: Piet van Weel
> Ok, I gotta ask.
> How much is fiber to the house costing you?

$100/mo for the circuit and the ISP services.  That's for business-grade
service, which permits me to (legally) run servers and use it for commercial
purposes.  Non-commercial service is available for less.

> From: "slinkymax0r" <slinkywizard at integraonline.com>
> 2) Test Your Firewall Locally against another system on your lan, using
> NFS preferably, to verify that you can get that amount of throughput
> bi-directionally over ethernet. This little sanity check might save you a
> lot of grief.
>
> I'm assuming that you're running the tests directly from the firewall, and
> not from clients behind the firewall.  If that is not the case, let me
> know.

#2 is an obvious idea, but one I overlooked before posting.  In fact,
Charlie's test proved everything was fine, but I should have checked that
first. 

And yes, I was testing from the firewall.  All is fine there as we learned,
but my individual client workstations are still running poorly.  That issue
is still to be researched.

> From: "Vincent Yau"
> With DSL/Cable modem, they usually don't like you running
> services like sshd, web site etc.. that would allow incoming
> traffic.
> Is Verizon FIOS also take the same stance?  Or they really
> don't care?    Can I run sshd and copy files back home
> from work?  (Assuming I will install a proper firewall) Just curious.

See my comments above about business-grade vs. non-commercial service.  It
depends on what you sign up for and how much you pay.  I can't speak to how
strictly they're enforcing their TOS.

And thanks to Wil and everyone else for the great links.

              -Brian Martin

Subject: [PLUG] Vim scripting problem
Message-ID: <20050913031318.95874.qmail@web60811.mail.yahoo.com>

Hi all,

Is there any way, in a vim script, to get "dollar digit" variables such
as those used in Perl to extract matched text? I have the following:

  function GotoCFile()
    if search('^\s*#\s*cfile:\s*\(\w\+\)')

      " $1 is not the variable in vim
      exe "echo 'Found ".$1."'"
    else
      exe "echo 'Could not find name of corresponding file'"
    end
  endfunction

I'm trying to find a Perl comment like this:

    # cfile: somefile

And assign "somefile" to a variable. I've searched all over the Vim
documentation for this and it seems to involve finding the starting and
ending positions of the match and doing a bit of math and a extracting
a substring. Tell me it's not this hard!

Cheers,
Ovid

From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)

Subject: [PLUG] Vim scripting problem
In-Reply-To: <20050913031318.95874.qmail@web60811.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20050913031318.95874.qmail@web60811.mail.yahoo.com>

# from Ovid
# on Monday 12 September 2005 08:13 pm:

>Is there any way, in a vim script, to get "dollar digit" variables
> such as those used in Perl to extract matched text? I have the
> following:

Doesn't look likely, but you could try the gvim gurus on irc or 
something.  I've had them answer strange questions immediately after 
hours of searching the docs.

This is slightly enlightening to your situation (at least it tells you 
not to expect too much.)

:help search
  "When a match has been found its line number is returned, and
    the cursor will be positioned at the match."


>  function GotoCFile()
>    if search('^\s*#\s*cfile:\s*\(\w\+\)')

Sounds like your best bet is to go with the fact that the cursor is now 
at the beginning of the matching line.

Maybe substitute() (which interestingly enough leads to the docs for 
submatch() and that happens to be just close enough to what you need to 
make you really frustrated at the infinite ad-hoc development :-)

>I've searched all over the Vim
>documentation for this and it seems to involve finding the starting
> and ending positions of the match and doing a bit of math and a
> extracting a substring. Tell me it's not this hard!

Why would you expect otherwise?  I guess you can try using vim-perl.  If 
not, you should expect more of the same.

From aaron at bavariati.org  Tue Sep 13 11:06:15 2005
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Tue, 13 Sep 2005 11:06:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] A Wine Question
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509131047400.9398@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509131047400.9398@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20050913180615.GJ15033@bavariati.org>

On Tue, Sep 13, 2005 at 10:51:15AM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   I have Wine-20050831 installed here, and configured correctly ... I think.
> Since I could not run the application off the cdrom, I copied all that to an
> nfs-mounted filesystem and tried running it from within the designated "c:\"
> drive in my ~/. Here's what I see:
> 
> [rshepard at salmo ~/wine/drives/c]$ wine /mnt/usr4/MapSource/Setup.exe 
> wine: cannot find '/mnt/usr4/MapSource/Setup.exe'

Well, for Wine to work, it needs to be able to map a Windows drive to
the path.  Maybe it doesn't know how, in this case.

I've always had good luck just changing to the directory where the
Windows binary is and running from there.

But really, it should be able to run from the CD-ROM anyway.  That's a
red flag right there.

Have you found any info anywhere on whether anyone else has tried
running MapSource under Wine?  I'd be quite surprised if you were the
first.  It might even be listed on the WineHQ compatibility database.




From creswick at gmail.com  Tue Sep 13 11:14:20 2005
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Tue, 13 Sep 2005 14:14:20 -0400
Subject: [PLUG] A Wine Question
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509131047400.9398@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509131047400.9398@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec805091311141ee72f6e@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/13/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    I have Wine-20050831 installed here, and configured correctly ... I think.
> Since I could not run the application off the cdrom, I copied all that to an
> nfs-mounted filesystem and tried running it from within the designated "c:\"
> drive in my ~/. Here's what I see:
> 
> [rshepard at salmo ~/wine/drives/c]$ wine /mnt/usr4/MapSource/Setup.exe
> wine: cannot find '/mnt/usr4/MapSource/Setup.exe'

AFAIK, wine expects a path with respect to the fake windows drives it
knows about.  Most likely, wine configured your cdrom to show up as a
D: drive, your home directory to be Z: or some such, and so on.  It
probably did *not* enable access to /mnt/usr4, and therefore can't
find the file.

Did you try running it off of the cdrom? (wine /mnt/cdrom/Setup.exe) 
If that fails with the same error, try copying it into ~/wine/drives/c
and running it out of there.  Alternatively, check your wine config,
and setup /mnt/usr4 to be a fake windows drive, and call it H or
something.

--Rogan



> 
>    I've read the FAQ and docs on the WineHQ Web site and cannot figure out
> what I've missed. Of course, it's possible that the application just will not
> install, but I'd like to be sure that I've tried properly before giving up.
> 
>    All suggestions welcome.

It was from the latter directory (that contains the windows/ file tree) that
I invoked wine; not from the directory with the application.

On Tue, 13 Sep 2005, Rogan Creswick wrote:

> AFAIK, wine expects a path with respect to the fake windows drives it knows
> about. Most likely, wine configured your cdrom to show up as a D: drive,
> your home directory to be Z: or some such, and so on. It probably did *not*
> enable access to /mnt/usr4, and therefore can't find the file.

Rogan,

   Here's the pertinent sections of ~/.wine/config and ~/.wine/dosdrives:

[Drive C]
"Path" = "/home/rshepard/wine/drives/c/"
"Type" = "hd"
"Label" = "MS-DOS"
"Filesystem" = "win95"

[Drive D]
"Path" = "/mnt/cdrom"
"Type" = "cdrom"
"Label" = "CD-Rom"
"Filesystem" = "win95"
; make sure that device is correct and has proper permissions !
"Device" = "/dev/cdrom"

   and,

lrwxrwxrwx  1 rshepard users 28 2005-09-13 10:44 c: -> /home/rshepard/wine/drives/c/

> Did you try running it off of the cdrom? (wine /mnt/cdrom/Setup.exe)

   Yes. This is what I tried first.

> If that fails with the same error, try copying it into ~/wine/drives/c and
> running it out of there. Alternatively, check your wine config, and setup
> /mnt/usr4 to be a fake windows drive, and call it H or something.

   This application cdrom holds 587M of data.

   Thinking that wine might not like nfs-mounted filesystems, I moved the
cdrom image from /mnt/usr4/MapSource to a local filesystm, /data1/MapSource.
No difference in trying to install the app.

   I'll try calling /data1/MapSource/ drive e:\ and see if that makes any
difference. Then I give up and beat on VMware again for upgrade support.

Thanks,

Rich

> cd /mnt/cdrom/

Rogan,

   Here's an example that mimics what you wrote:

[rshepard at salmo /data1/MapSource]$ wine Setup.exe 
Warning: could not find DOS drive for current working directory
'/data1/MapSource', starting in the Windows directory.
wine: cannot find 'Setup.exe'

   In ~/.wine/config I have:

[Drive F]
"Path" = "/data1/Mapsource/"
"Type" = "hd"
"Label" = "Home"
"Filesystem" = "win95"

   So, from within ~/wine/drives/c/windows:

[rshepard at salmo ~/wine/drives/c/windows]$ wine /data1/MapSource/Setup.exe 
wine: cannot find '/data1/MapSource/Setup.exe'

and

[rshepard at salmo ~/wine/drives/c/windows]$ wine f:\Setup.exe
wine: cannot find 'f:Setup.exe'

   So, there must be something in the setup or configuration that I've missed
here.

Rich

>>>>> "Rich" == Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> writes:


AB> http://www.aircable.net/AIRcable/serialUSB.html

Rich>    This looks good, but for one catch: the need for 100 VAC on
Rich> the serial device side. That eliminates this solution for
Rich> digital cameras, GPS receivers, and the like.

Rich>    It is an interesting approach, however.

We've been doing some work with a Free2Move serial-bluetooth dongle.
You just need to provide 5VDC to the dongle.  The USB dongle, a
linksys device, laptop-side runs off of USB power.

  <http://www.free2move.se/prod_spp.htm>

We put together a Class 1 (100m range) bluetooth kit for about $300
for all parts including a nice Li-Ion camcorder battery that will
power the dongle for about 48 hours/charge.  Works great.  We *needed*
electrical isolation though, so wireless was a requirement.

I am sure you can do cheaper though.  I bought what amounted to a
USB-serial adapter from Radio Shack for ~$22 and some solder, though
it was for use with 3.3V serial signal levels as a console cable to a
Netgear WGT634u.

From 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com  Tue Sep 13 15:21:25 2005
From: 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com (Steve Bonds)
Date: Tue, 13 Sep 2005 15:21:25 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] determining files associated with particular sectors?
In-Reply-To: <29283-03649@sneakemail.com>
References: <868xypi13e.fsf@coulee.tdb.com> <29283-03649@sneakemail.com>
Message-ID: <15329-86783@sneakemail.com>

On 8/26/05, I wrote:
> On 26 Aug 2005 00:55:33 -0700, Russell Senior seniorr-at-aracnet.com
> |PDX Linux| <...> wrote:
> >
> > Is there a utility that will tell me, given a sector number within a
> > device, which file is allocated to that sector?  I am seeing a few bad
> > sectors on the old laptop drive (as I copy the files over) and want to
> > ensure I patch up any associated bitrot.
> 
> Russell:
> 
> Most disk forensics programs will do this.  Since the disk is damaged
> you'll need to use "dd" with a couple options as described here:
> 
> http://www.forensicfocus.com/index.php?name=Forums&file=viewtopic&t=29

I recently had the good(?) fortune to have to extract as much data as
I could from a dying drive.  In the process I found a much better way
to pull the data off than plain dd.

There is a script called dd_rhelp included in Kurt Garloff's
"ddrescue" package (http://www.garloff.de/kurt/linux/ddrescue/) that
does a great job of pulling data off damaged/flaky drives.  It
provides nice real-time statistics on the data recovery process and is
simple to use.  ("dd_rhelp <source drive> <destination file>")

This is not to be confused with the GNU software "ddrescue" which is
similar, but has not been updated recently and does not include
dd_rhelp.

On thing I also noticed-- if your drive has any Linux RAID autodetect
partitions (unlikely on a laptop) the Linux kernel will try to
activate/re-sync them on startup.  I think the RAID identifiers will
prevent it from re-syncing over the top of the data you're trying to
save-- but it's possible for this to go awry, trashing the data you're
trying to save.

That's when a hardware write blocker might be handy to borrow from
someone (or purchase for about $300 if desperate...)

  -- Steve

wine f:/Setup.exe

or 

wine "f:\Setup.exe"

as an escape char.)

binary, so you don't have to debug the pathing any more than you
absolutely must.

windows drive names.  This is what I've done in the past with success
when dealing with wine.

apologise for beating a dead horse.

On Sep 13, 2005, at 8:22 PM, Matt Alexander wrote:
> Is anyone out there working in a position that deals primarily with
> computer/network security?

Me, for one. I don't have all my NDA's in front of me, but I guess it's 
safe to say that I spend a lot of my time working on network and 
host-attack security issues for three-letter-agencies.

>  If so, how did you get into the field?

I worked in so many aspects of computing and networking that I had 
gained the needed knowledge base for the work I'm doing, and I didn't 
get burnt out or pigeonholed into one specific area that prevented me 
from doing "bigger picture" work and continuing to grow my knowledge 
and skills.

>  What
> education or certifications do you feel are worthwhile for a career in
> computer security?

Well, it's a *huge* field.... you might have to narrow down the 
question a bit. There's alphabet soup out there covering all the 
different aspects, and it's far beyond anything one human could learn 
in a lifetime....

For example, somebody specializing in designing hardware encryption 
devices needs a totally different background than somebody specializing 
in writing corporate security policy, and somebody researching the 
detection of network threats needs a different background than somebody 
focusing on social engineering....

Is there a specific area you're looking into?

-Ron Chmara

From nickaubert at gmail.com  Wed Sep 14 00:32:10 2005
From: nickaubert at gmail.com (Nick Aubert)
Date: Wed, 14 Sep 2005 00:32:10 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Vim scripting problem
In-Reply-To: <200509122237.25436.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <20050913031318.95874.qmail@web60811.mail.yahoo.com>
	<200509122237.25436.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <55ef240d050914003264ff91e5@mail.gmail.com>

When using vim as an editor, the matched patterns in parenthesis are
called up by escaping the number with a backslash instead of a dollar
symbol.

The perl regexp

s/(foo)(bar)/$2$1/

would be

s/\(foo\)\(bar\)/\2\1/

This works for vim in editing mode.  I don't know how much different
the behavior is with vim scripts.  The relevant document on my Gentoo
system is

/usr/share/vim/vim63/doc/change.txt

You might want to look for that file somewhere equivalent on your
system if you're still working on this problem.

-Nick

From rsteff at comcast.net  Wed Sep 14 12:07:03 2005
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Wed, 14 Sep 2005 12:07:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Javascript Array Problem
Message-ID: <432874D7.4010809@comcast.net>

I'm working on adding a category of images to my photo website. (The 
problem described below is on my machine at home, and not up on the web, 
yet, so I'll just have to describe it.)

I have a set of arrays to hold information about the images in each 
category, and I build them with the following javascript code:

//===================================================================
   // Function to initialize an array of n entries
   function MakeArray(n) {
     this.length = n
     for (var i = 1; i <= n; i++) {
       this[i] = 0
     }
     return this
   }


//=================================================================
   // constructor function for array elements
   function img(code, caption, qty0, qty1, qty2, qty3, qty4) {
     this.code = code
     this.caption = caption
     this.qty0 = 0
     this.qty1 = 0
     this.qty2 = 0
     this.qty3 = 0
     this.qty4 = 0
     }

//======================================================================
function buildPortlandArray() {
   portlandArray[1] = new img("P0001", "Oregon City Bridge")
   portlandArray[2] = new img("P0002", "End of the Oregon Trail")
   portlandArray[3] = new img("P0003", "Sacagawea - Washington Park")
   portlandArray[4] = new img("P0004", "Pittock Mansion, Portland, OR")
   portlandArray[5] = new img("P0005", "Vista House, Crown Point")
   }

//===================================================================
function initializeSite() {
// other things happen in this function, but I cut out most of it for
// the sake of brevity

   var portlandImageCount = 5

   portlandArray = MakeArray(portlandImageCount)
   buildPortlandArray()
   }

In the category I'm trying to add with buildPortlandArray, the first two 
entries of the array return as "undefined" but the last three work just 
fine. There are four other arrays on the site that all work as expected.

Has anyone ever seen something like this before, where the first two 
entries in an array don't work, but the last three do?

Thanks for any ideas you may have to offer.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

Note Cards & Photographic Prints available at:
http://home.comcast.net/~rcsphoto/

From ckonstan at lunarlogic.com  Wed Sep 14 13:49:54 2005
From: ckonstan at lunarlogic.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Wed, 14 Sep 2005 13:49:54 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Javascript Array Problem
In-Reply-To: <20050914201412.4FF3B4014@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20050914201412.4FF3B4014@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0509141338010.7439@localhost>

Javascript arrays are sparse.  They're not really numerical-indexed
arrays, they're more like associative arrays.  It's still good form to
start them at 0, but it's not a bug to start at 1.

I believe function MakeArray() is unnecessary.  You do not have to
pre-pad an array in javascript.  Just use:

     var newArray = new Array();
     newArray[1] = "the value of 1";
     newArray[37] = "the value of 37";

Note the large skip in indices.  Perfectly legal in javascript.  This is
what I mean by sparse.

Or you could opt for a real associative array, with strings for keys:

     var newAlist = new Object();
     newAlist["one"] = "the value of one";
     newAlist["thirtyseven"] = "the value of thirtyseven";

though this has nothing to do with the current problem.  Just an
observation to help unserstanding of js arrays.  an Array() is an
associative array with numbers for keys, though they can be any numbers,
and not even consecutive.  An Object() can be used as a string-keyed
associative array.

I still say all bets are off until some semicolons get put in.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Wed Sep 14 13:59:58 2005
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Wed, 14 Sep 2005 13:59:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Javascript Array Problem
In-Reply-To: <20050914201412.4FF3B4014@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20050914201412.4FF3B4014@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <43288F4E.4070502@comcast.net>

Bill Ensley wrote:
> My javascript is rusty, but shouldn't this array start at 0?

While the first entry in the array is position 0, that entry contains 
the number of the rest of the entries in the array. I got this bit of 
cleverness from Danny Goodman's JavaScript Handbook. As he explains:

"If you look at the MakeArray() function closely, you'll notice that the 
first entry(row) of the array is assigned not only a value (the number 
of rows of the whole array), but also the name(length). This first 
entry, which has an index value of 0, can now be accessed by your script 
with what appears to be a familiar array length property reference:

arrayLen = myArray.length

"Unlike JavaScript's own objects and arrays, then, the 0 index of these 
transient arrays should not contain any real data..."

> //===================================================================
>    // Function to initialize an array of n entries
>    function MakeArray(n) {
>      this.length = n
>      for (var i = 1; i <= n; i++) {
>        this[i] = 0
>      }
>      return this
>    }

So, Bill, you are right, but not for this example.

From mikeraz at patch.com  Thu Sep 15 08:04:11 2005

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Thu Sep 15 08:27:18 2005
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Thu, 15 Sep 2005 08:27:18 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] /dev/null for a login shell
In-Reply-To: <43281.170.135.241.45.1126796651.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
References: <43281.170.135.241.45.1126796651.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
Message-ID: <200509150827.18964.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Michael Rasmussen
# on Thursday 15 September 2005 08:04 am:

>However, can you use the mechanism for running binaries with a
> specific ID?

By "mechanism", do you mean changing the login shell entry?  Yeah.  You 
can put whatever you want in there.

I have my notebook setup like this:

#########
$ grep ^ex /etc/passwd
ex::1000:1000:Nobody,Nowhere,,:/home/ex:/usr/local/bin/be_ewilhelm

$ cat /usr/local/bin/be_ewilhelm
#!/bin/sh
su - ewilhelm startx
#########

So, instead of starting a display manager, it boots to a login prompt.  
The problem with logging in as a user and then running startx is that 
someone can Alt+F1 and kill X to get your login even if you locked the 
X display.  If you do that with this setup, it drops you back to the 
login prompt because the "ex" user's shell exited.

I didn't exactly grok part 2 of your question, but maybe the above means 
yes.

--Eric

From jmknoble+plug at pobox.com  Thu Sep 15 14:55:13 2005
From: jmknoble+plug at pobox.com (Jim Knoble)
Date: Thu, 15 Sep 2005 17:55:13 -0400
Subject: [PLUG] /dev/null for a login shell
In-Reply-To: <55555.170.135.241.45.1126797985.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
References: <43281.170.135.241.45.1126796651.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
	<55555.170.135.241.45.1126797985.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
Message-ID: <20050915215513.GA30624@crawfish.ais.com>

Circa 2005-09-15 11:26 dixit Michael Rasmussen:

: 
: Michael Rasmussen said:
: > I know you can use /dev/null as a login shell for user accounts ...
: > However, can you use the mechanism for running binaries with a specific
: > ID?
: 
: Bah, nevermind.  I found a good example with http running as nobody on my
: work Sparc.  And in this environment nobody has no shell.

If you mean that no shell is listed for user 'nobody', then that usually
means "use the default shell", generally /bin/sh.  For example, the
following line in /etc/passwd gives 'nobody' the shell '/bin/sh':

    nobody:*:32767:32767:Unprivileged user:/:

What you really want is either an invalid shell (like /dev/null,
suggested above), or, better, a shell which does nothing.  For example:

    nobody:*:32767:32767:Unprivileged user:/:/bin/false

This works best if /bin/false is a statically linked binary on your
system, not a shell script which exits nonzero, as it is on some
systems.  The following C code will produce a reasonably safe and
compact statically linked /bin/false:

    #include <unistd.h>
    int main(int argc, char **argv) {
        _exit(1);
    }

Cheers.

-- 
jim knoble  |  jmknoble at pobox.com  |  http://www.pobox.com/~jmknoble/
(GnuPG fingerprint: 809F:09B9:9686:D035:4AB0::9455:124B:0A62:DD6A:76D6)

Date: Fri, 16 Sep 2005 13:22:04 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Announcement: Linux Clinic this Saturday
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509161316550.26832@f2it.com>


           *** Portland Linux User's Group (PLUG) Announcement ***

              The Linux Clinic - third Saturday of every month

     Interested in Linux? Need help installing or configuring Linux? Be
            sure to attend the next session of the Linux Clinic.

                                Linux Clinic
                              September 17, 2005
                                 1PM - 4PM

                             Riverdale High School
                           9727 SW Terwilliger Blvd.
                            Portland, Oregon 97219

    What to bring:
      * Your computer, monitor, keyboard and mouse.
      * Any relevant peripherals (printer, modem, etc).
      * Your Linux distribution (if you have already installed).
      * The manuals for your hardware - especially your monitor manual.

    Resources available:
      * Volunteers skilled in Linux installation and maintenance.
      * The PLUG CD library (including several recent Linux
        distributions).
      * T-1 Internet connection
      * phone jacks for testing modem and Internet setups.
      * Ethernet drops.
      * Infocus projector for demonstrations.
      * An extra Linux box for experimenting.
      * A number of reference materials

    For directions to the school see:
    http://www.riverdale.k12.or.us/info/direct.html

    For questions regarding the Clinics email Dan Haskell at:
    dhaskell at acm.org

    For information about PLUG or the Clinics see:
    http://www.pdxlinux.org

	<1126852628.6435.3.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <55ef240d050916181782a0c2a@mail.gmail.com>

This may be slightly off-topic, but while we're on the subject of
bashing Nautilus, the September 6 episode of Cringely's NerdTV has an
interview of Apple developer Andy Hertzfeld in which he talks about
the development of Nautilus, and his disappointment with what became
of the project.  Basically, the original aspirations of Nautilus were
much more ambitious: it was to be a "graphical shell," a new kind of
interface.  After the initial project ran out of funding, another
group took it over and turned into a clone of the Windows desktop
explorer.  I thought it was a good show.  It's available for regular
download or bittorrent from pbs.org/nerdtv.

-Nick

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Sat Sep 17 19:12:27 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Sat, 17 Sep 2005 19:12:27 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ThinkPad 600E Indicator Light
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509171858080.18587@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509171619300.9161@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<86zmqbrx6c.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0509171858080.18587@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <432CCD0B.3080301@axpr.net>

Rich Shepard wrote:
>   No. I don't have one. I suppose that we could attach one on top of the
> existing keyboard with velcro and it would be less expensive than having
> the
> defective keyboard (assuming that's what it is) replaced.

FWIW, I've replaced a few laptop keyboards... it's usually not very
difficult to accomplish.  A new keyboard can often be had for around $70
or so, and installation on a laptop you're not familiar with could take
maybe a half hour or so.

Usually you remove obvious screws, pop off the piece of plastic between
the keyboard and the screen, remove another screw or two, flip the
keyboard up, pull out the ribbon cable, remove old keyboard, and do
exactly the opposite with the new keyboard.

Rob

On Mon, Sep 19, 2005 at 10:30:25AM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   OK. Later this evening. It is not obvious to me that a loose connection
> would affect only some keys, but what the heck ... it'll be a test drive of
> my ability to take it apart and reassemble it so it works. Sorta'. :-)
> 
>   I've never before dinked with a portable, other than to replace a hard
> drive or add memory.

Well, the IBMs are about the nicest ones to work on.  Just make sure
you use screwdrivers that fit well (Philips screwdrivers come in
several sizes such as #2, #1, #0 and #00 and the wrong size will strip
a screw) and be careful about forcing things.

-- 
All parts should go together without forcing.  You must remember that
the parts you are reassembling were disassembled by you.  Therefore,
if you can't get them together again, there must be a reason.  
By all means, do not use a hammer.    -- IBM maintenance manual, 1925

If the keyboard scan signals travel over the keyboard connector, a
problem affecting only certain keys is quite possible.  OTOH, if the
microcontroller that scans the keyboard is part of the keyboard assembly,
it's very unlikely that you have a simple connector problem.

galen

Rod,

   If this is still not a smooth transition, why not leave it for later and do
the easy things first? For example, convert the servers to linux and use
samba to support those who must remain with certain Microsoft applications.
In the meantime, phase in OO.o and other office applications in their
Microsoft and Apple incarnations so staff become used to working with those
tools.

   Yesterday, a friend in Boulder who has to keep running Microsoft because so
many of his clients still do that, told me that he cannot keep XP running for
more than 1.5 hours at a time, even when he's using only Access and some VBA
scripts. In my naivete, I would think that managers would prefer to forego a
calendaring application to gain the ability to keep the systems up and not
have to take forced coffee breaks for rebooting during the work day.

Rich

Rod,

   The saddest part of this whole situation is that all the data they use are
freely available on government Web sites. I've been getting the latest
spatial data for years from state and federal agencies. If the companies
whose entire business is the sale of maps and the devices to display them
cannot provide either current data or the tools to convert from open standard
sources to their proprietary, internal formats, then we're part of the
problem by not making enough noise.

> I hope you have better luck with Garmin.  If you do I know what my next GPS
> unit will be.

   My sub-survey grade GPS (+/- 30 cm accuracy with real-time differential
corrections from the USGS beacon transmitters) has mission planning and
post-processing software that works only on Microsoft. The company won't
consider supporting linux even though it's only a relatively simple port.

Richard Shepard:
I've found that some flat cables in laptops use a zero insertion force 
(ZIF) connector.  This can be tricky to connect.  Look for a movable 
part at the end (the open end) of the connector.

# from MJang
# on Monday 19 September 2005 08:50 pm:

>Just wondering if the upcoming Government Open Source Conference
>(GOSCON) might be worth the time and trouble (www.goscon.org) - is
>anyone here familiar with the quality of the sessions? Will it be half
>as good as the regular OSCON?

Good?  You mean tasty technical details about cutting-edge projects that 
were first posted one week before the conference?  No.

As I understand it, this is a totally different beast, meant mostly to 
give government IT guys a rundown on how and why to use open source in 
their everyday problem-solving.  Did you hear something different?

Sure, these guys are a technical audience and there will probably be 
several sessions of nitty-gritty, but I don't think that Larry Wall is 
coming.  You also probably shouldn't expect to see anything like 
Damian's "how to build a beowulf out of laserjets" talk.

--Eric

understandability all come down strongly in favor of open source
solutions to complex problems of any sort.

Hi:

Recently, I brought up to athlon64 box.  They both run great (most of the 
time).
The slower of the two, however, seems to be exhibiting some weird
behavior at times.  They are all sorts of them here and there
and I am slowly tackling one at a time.

This one, however, is a little odd for me.
When I run top,   I can always see that under "CPU states", the "softirq" 
category always
hovers around 50% usage.  Even when I am not running anything (other than
what is minimum to have the box running), it would be 50% idle and 50% 
softirq.
I don't see this on any other boxes so I am wondering what is special about 
this.
Can someone tell me what is softirq?

I am running kernel 2.4.21-32.EL  64-bit version

Thanks for any tip/pointer.

--Vincent

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Wed Sep 21 12:07:50 2005
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Wed, 21 Sep 2005 12:07:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] What is softirq?
In-Reply-To: <BAY108-F4E1148E91C6269F6A8FFFB8940@phx.gbl>
References: <BAY108-F4E1148E91C6269F6A8FFFB8940@phx.gbl>
Message-ID: <c295378405092112075742eef9@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/21/05, Vincent Yau <v_yau3 at hotmail.com> wrote:
> Hi:
>
> Recently, I brought up to athlon64 box.  They both run great (most of the
> time).
> The slower of the two, however, seems to be exhibiting some weird
> behavior at times.  They are all sorts of them here and there
> and I am slowly tackling one at a time.
>
> This one, however, is a little odd for me.
> When I run top,   I can always see that under "CPU states", the "softirq"
> category always
> hovers around 50% usage.  Even when I am not running anything (other than
> what is minimum to have the box running), it would be 50% idle and 50%
> softirq.
> I don't see this on any other boxes so I am wondering what is special about
> this.
> Can someone tell me what is softirq?
>
> I am running kernel 2.4.21-32.EL  64-bit version
>
> Thanks for any tip/pointer.

softirqs are software interrupts, used often by device drivers to do
further processing of something outside of the hardware interrupt
handler, which needs to run as fast as possible so interrupts can be
re-enabled.  Perhaps you have some rogue hardware?  Does
/proc/interrupts show something going crazy?  Is the box receiving a
lot of network traffic?  Anything in your logs?

I found this guy's posting, perhaps you should try the "noapic" option
to see if that fixes it, then file a bug with RedHat.

http://kerneltrap.org/node/5534

Jason

From gilmanhunt at comcast.net  Wed Sep 21 17:12:42 2005
From: gilmanhunt at comcast.net (Russ Gilman-Hunt)
Date: Wed, 21 Sep 2005 17:12:42 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DNS question
Message-ID: <20050922001242.GA9894@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>

I'm sorry to trouble all of you for an answer to a silly question.

Is there an automated way for a DNS master to tell a DNS slave "now ask me
for this new domain, so you can be authorative too" when a new domain is
configured on the master?

I'm looking at two servers, one of which does an automatic hosting sort of
thing and uses some scripts to create the named.conf file and the entries in
/var/named/ . It needs to share this information with some slaves.

While I can create a fairly trivial set of scripts to communicate the new
domain back and forth, I'm sure this wheel has been invented before.

I eagerly await your discussion.

Russ
(not Russ Senior)


From mikeraz at patch.com  Wed Sep 21 22:25:16 2005
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Wed, 21 Sep 2005 22:25:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DNS question
In-Reply-To: <20050922001242.GA9894@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>
References: <20050922001242.GA9894@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20050922052516.GC6626@patch.com>

Russ Gilman-Hunt wrote:
> Is there an automated way for a DNS master to tell a DNS slave "now ask me
> for this new domain, so you can be authorative too" when a new domain is
> configured on the master?

When the zone is reloaded, say through `rndc reload` the master will
sent an alert to the secondaries with the new serial number.  Note the
secondaries are under no compulsion to do anything about the matter.

From ed at alcpress.com  Wed Sep 21 23:13:13 2005
From: ed at alcpress.com (Ed Sawicki)
Date: Wed, 21 Sep 2005 23:13:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DNS question
In-Reply-To: <20050922001242.GA9894@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>
References: <20050922001242.GA9894@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>
Message-ID: <43324B79.8080405@alcpress.com>

Russ Gilman-Hunt wrote:
> I'm sorry to trouble all of you for an answer to a silly question.
> 
> Is there an automated way for a DNS master to tell a DNS slave "now ask me
> for this new domain, so you can be authorative too" when a new domain is
> configured on the master?

BIND zone transfer protocol only transfers information for zones that have
already been configured at both ends. It does not have a way to
instruct/request a server to become authoritative for a new zone. You'll
have to do this out of band.


> I'm looking at two servers, one of which does an automatic hosting sort of
> thing and uses some scripts to create the named.conf file and the entries in
> /var/named/ . It needs to share this information with some slaves.

If you have administrative control of your slaves, why not forget about
zone transfers and push everything out to the slaves - including new
configuration information?


> While I can create a fairly trivial set of scripts to communicate the new
> domain back and forth, I'm sure this wheel has been invented before.

It has. It's scp.

Ed Sawicki


> I eagerly await your discussion.

> Russ
> (not Russ Senior)

From ed at alcpress.com  Thu Sep 22 10:36:10 2005
From: ed at alcpress.com (Ed Sawicki)
Date: Thu, 22 Sep 2005 10:36:10 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: DNS question
In-Reply-To: <20050922151852.GA11892@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>
References: <20050922001242.GA9894@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>	<43324B79.8080405@alcpress.com>
	<20050922151852.GA11892@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>
Message-ID: <4332EB8A.3010001@alcpress.com>

Russ Gilman-Hunt wrote:
> You and YAR (heh) have the right of it. 
> Basically, we have one host ( configured as a DNS master ) where new domains
> can be created with users purchasing domains and hosting options. When a new
> domain is created, it creates the master entry on this server, but I need to
> notify the slaves that there is indeed a new domain for which they need the
> configuration file.
> 
> Ed, are you suggesting ( below ) that I use scp to copy the domain info
> between the DNS servers and not use the master/slave routine?

Yes. Why be stuck using an antique, protocol-specific file transfer
protocol, when a much nicer and more secure generic protocol (SSL/scp)
can be used. Plus, your application is crying out for push - not pull.
BIND is pull-oriented.

Besides, you have to move more than zone files to your slaves - you have
to update configuration files - something that BIND zone transfers doesn't
provide for. If you're going to roll your own stuff for the configuration,
handle the zone files as well.


  I thought that
> this was what DNS master/slave stuff was for- whether or not one had
> administrative control over the servers

The only time you MUST use BIND's zone transfer protocol is when
your slave servers are not under your control and you can't convince
the administrators to let you scp your zone files. Otherwise, BIND
servers don't really care how their zone files are updated. Of course,
if you do update your BIND zone files using scp, you'll have to signal
BIND that you've done this so it can reload them. While you could use
BIND 9's rndc program to signal BIND, I would just use ssh for that.


  ( I can see that it'd be easier for
> people who have one or the other ).









> -Russ GH
>

From ed at alcpress.com  Thu Sep 22 12:20:09 2005
From: ed at alcpress.com (Ed Sawicki)
Date: Thu, 22 Sep 2005 12:20:09 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DNS question
In-Reply-To: <5.1.0.14.2.20050922103528.0209de78@www.hotsushi.com>
References: <20050922001242.GA9894@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>	<20050922001242.GA9894@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>
	<5.1.0.14.2.20050922103528.0209de78@www.hotsushi.com>
Message-ID: <433303E9.2020006@alcpress.com>

Mike Neal wrote:
> 
>>
>> If you have administrative control of your slaves, why not forget about
>> zone transfers and push everything out to the slaves - including new
>> configuration information?
> 
> 
> Hi Ed,
> 
> Just to understand the idea, are you thinking disable zone transfers for 
> security and fully synchronize using something like SCP to copy 
> directories?

We have two discussions interwoven in this thread. The first is how to
tell a remote server that it must now handle (be authoritative for)
additional zones (domains). The second is replacing BIND's zone transfer
protocol with something else. I've suggested a way to handle both, but
it may be confusing so let's focus on the second - replacing the BIND
zone transfer protocol. Understanding this will make understanding
the first task easier.

Suppose you have three DNS servers running BIND now. Suppose one is
configured to be the master of all your zones. The other two servers
are configured as slaves. You have administrative control over all
three servers - you can change BIND configuration files, you can
install scripts, you can run ssh, scp, etc. You might be root on all
three.

The general idea is to reconfigure the two slave servers so they are
masters for all the zones. The exact same zone files can be used on
all three servers. Use scp to securely push the zone files from the
"master" to the other masters. Whenever you make changes to any zone
file, send that zone file to the other two servers using scp. Automate
this however you like - a Makefile, a script, etc.

Whenever you update a zone file you must tell BIND about it so it
can reload it. With BIND9, you do something like "rndc reload...".
Your makefile or script will do this for all three servers. You'll
pass these commands to ssh for the two remote servers.

You no longer have to remember to update zone serial numbers - unless
they server a purpose for your administrative needs.
The changes take place immediately. You can turn off BIND features
that are no longer necessary, like NOTIFY.

Getting back to the original poster's task of having remote DNS
servers become aware that they must be authoritative for new
zones:

Whenever you update the master's configuration file to add or delete
zones, you push the modified configuration file to the two remote
servers and send them the command (via ssh) that forces BIND to reread
its configuration.

DNS doesn't have to be as mysterious and complicated as the
DNS priesthood have made it.

Ed



> 
> Thanks,
> Mike

Now, for the question you really asked.  Unfortunately, Garmin doesn't have
any kind of custom map utility and hasn't published the specs of the
MapSource format.

There are third parties out there who have developed the ability to load
custom maps into various Garmin units, though. These are not authorized or
supported by Garmin in any fashion, but you might conduct an Internet search
on the topic, via some search engine like Yahoo! or Google.

These are a couple I've run into...NOT an endorsement or a recommendation in
any way...but I have seen the websites, if you want to look:
http://www.gpsinformation.net/gpsmapper/gpsmapper.htm
http://www.gpstm.com/eng/dekode_eng.htm

From aaron at bavariati.org  Fri Sep 23 09:49:50 2005

On Fri, Sep 23, 2005 at 08:40:16AM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
> What is the stability status of the 2.6 kernel series? I just learned that
> Emperor Linux now supports only this series, and not the 2.4 series.

Good for them.

> Patrick is still keeping the 2.4 kernels in the main distribution,
> with 2.6 as an option.

Well, 2.6.0 was released as a "stable" kernel Dec 18, 2003.  The OSDL
has been happily running 2.6 on its production systems ever since.

I'm sure you've heard a lot of guff about new development models
making 2.6 less stable.  What's happened, as far as I know, is that
development has moved into patched versions that are *based* on 2.6.
If you're running mainline 2.6, it's stable.

If you're running laptops, you should've moved to 2.6 long ago; the
driver and power-management support are greatly improved.  If you're
running servers, you should've moved to 2.6 long ago; the network and
filesystem performance are both much better.  If you're doing compute-
intensive stuff, you should've moved to 2.6 long ago; the new CPU
schedulers are great, and the x64 and hyperthreading support is nice.

>   What kernel (built here from source) would you experts recommend for my
> Slackware systems? 

Built from source? Newest 2.6.  I'd start playing with a 2.6.14pre
version because 2.6.14 should be out soon.  Any version from 2.6.10 up
is pretty good.

But heck, if you're running a decently-maintained distro, just
use the latest precompiled kernel from that.  It's tested, and the
ACPI and sonypi drivers should be good enough.  You'll need the new
module-init-tools, is all.

Can't wait for 2.7,
  Aaron

conversion utility doesn't work properly on the kernels and modules.

  Overall, the 2.6 kernel is *so* much better than the 2.4 tree that
you should run it, if you are able.  The 2.6 kernel is chock full of
"really neat stuff"(tm).

  From a practical standpoint, if your computer is running an old 2.4
based distro, then stick with a 2.4 kernel.  If you *need* a feature
or support that's only found in the 2.6 kernel tree, then upgrade your
machine to a 2.6 based distro.

  The pain and suffering involved in stuffing a 2.6 kernel into 2.4
based distro isn't worth the effort IMHO.  You can't just swap out the
kernel.  A lot of API and device driver stuff also changed.

Steve D...

Message-ID: <c2953784050923121115d5fb64@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/23/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
> On Fri, 23 Sep 2005, Eli Stair wrote:
>
> > The way I read it currently (as of a LKML post earlier this year) is that
> > 2.6.x are considered the "development" release, those with another
> > dot-release are the bugfixed/patched version of that.
> >
> > 2.6.13 - "development"
> > 2.6.13.2  - current "stable" (until 2.6.13.3)
>
> Eli, Aaron, et al.:
>
>    Please, let's not get wrapped around the axle on this, for the details are
> not of interest to my needs.
>
>    What I would greatly appreciate from those of you with much more kernel
> knowledge than I have is just _which_ 2.6.x kernel I should install for a
> stable, production, I-don't-want-to-pay-attention-to-it machine.

2.6.13 is not regarded as one of the more stable ones, but it is all a
matter of perspective.  SuSE uses 2.6.5 with a bunch of custom patches
for their enterprise line.  RedHat uses 2.6.9 for their enterprise
line.  Ubuntu uses 2.6.10.  Fedora (what I use) follows the current
mainline kernel fairly closely, except that they are avoiding 2.6.13
for the moment.

The kernel summit this summer addressed the perceived problem of
stability, based on the fact that kernel development is screaming
along at an incredible pace now with so many companies contributing. 
Basically, there's only two weeks after a stable release in which
Linus will accept new features from now on.  After that the kernel
hackers are (supposed) to devote time to testing and bugfixes.  See
http://kerneltrap.org/node/5500.

You (presumably as an end user) should be tracking one of two kernel
sources:  the distribution kernel for your distribution, or the
"stable" kernel series (i.e. 2.6.13.2 or something else in the 4-digit
version numbers).  The main reason is to get bugfixes and security
updates, something you're a little behind the times on if you're still
running 2.4.24.  If you really despise 2.6 kernels, at least stick
with the latest 2.4 kernel, since they are really not changing very
much anymore except for bugfixes and security updates.

Based on your "I-don't-want-to-pay-attention-to-it machine" statement,
I think you should use a distro kernel.

My $0.02,
Jason

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Fri Sep 23 14:23:16 2005
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Fri, 23 Sep 2005 14:23:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] serial console administration
In-Reply-To: <200509231336.21362.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200509231336.21362.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <f4468cae050923142334a1e76d@mail.gmail.com>

Couple of examples of why I've done it:

#1 You're using LinuxBIOS and never have local console on the
cluster/system, if you're not booted multiuser or networking is down
this is the only way.

#2 You're responsible for said systems, and something goes wrong while
you're NOT seated in the datacenter.  Without being able to get access
on the system, your choices for troubleshooting are severely limited
(think: you're on a ski vacation, or, you're not SUPPOSED to be on a
ski vacation but you are ;)

#3 Nothing is wrong with your system, but instead your network
hardware.  Without any access through you might not even be able to
determine if it was a PDU outage, a switch/router failure, a breaker
trip, etc.

Serial access is only something you think about when you don't have
it.  If you do, you can  find a way to solve a problem.  If you don't
have it, you will immediately see the need when the time comes. 
Obviously you don't plan on needing it, but there's ALWAYS Murphy's
Law :)

/eli

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Sep 23 14:27:08 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 23 Sep 2005 14:27:08 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] serial console administration
In-Reply-To: <200509231336.21362.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200509231336.21362.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.63.0509231415260.237@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Fri, 23 Sep 2005, Carla Schroder wrote:

> Is anyone using the serial console for more than the occasional 
> fix-of-last-resort? Since servers are presumably networked via 
> Ethernet, why would you routine server administration over a poky 
> ole serial line?

I've got most of my rack-mounts connected to a Digi CM 16, which 
provides serial console support.

Why do I use serial consoles?

1. I can replumb network connections -- or kill and completely restart
    sshd -- remotely.

2. I can reboot the machine remotely. Both grub and lilo will send
    output to ttySx if asked nicely.

3. One strand of cat-5 going to the serial port makes for a cleaner
    server room than a big (or three-fold) KVM cable.[1]

4. For SPARCs -- yeah, they're still around -- serial administration
    is actually easier than a full keyboard-mouse-monitor setup.
    (The same is true for Alphas, but I don't have any. Sniff.)

That said, I'd never do "routine server administration" over a serial 
line if Ethernet is available...

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

[1] Heinlein's law: A real server is fully functional with only
     three cables: power, ethernet, serial.

On Fri, Sep 23, 2005 at 10:53:30AM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   What I would greatly appreciate from those of you with much more kernel
> knowledge than I have is just _which_ 2.6.x kernel I should install for a
> stable, production, I-don't-want-to-pay-attention-to-it machine.

Yes, that's what you asked, and here's what I said before:

Built from source? Newest 2.6.  I'd start playing with a 2.6.14pre
version because 2.6.14 should be out soon.  Any version from 2.6.10 up
is pretty good.

But heck, if you're running a decently-maintained distro, just
use the latest precompiled kernel from that.  It's tested, and the
ACPI and sonypi drivers should be good enough.  You'll need the new
module-init-tools, is all.  

Just for laffs, try a stock Slackware 2.6 kernel.  The drivers you
need should be included, no custom kernel needed.

BTW, what did your vendor say?  How about Google?

Nobody here's paid to do this,
  Aaron

Subject: [PLUG] serial console administration

At 1:36 PM -0700 on 9/23/05, Carla Schroder wrote:
>Is anyone using the serial console for more than the occasional
>fix-of-last-resort? Since servers are presumably networked via Ethernet, why
>would you routine server administration over a poky ole serial line?
>
>Plus I eavesdropped shamelessly on a conversation about updating a Fedora box
>via the serial console, and it took an entire weekend. Yet the person who did
>it thought it was the greatest thing since twist-tops. Soooo... again, if
>Ethernet is working, why would do you this?
>
>--
>~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
>Carla Schroder
>http://www.tuxcomputing.com
>check out my new book, the "Linux Cookbook", the ultimate Linux user's
>and sysadmin's guide! http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/linuxckbk/
>this message brought to you
>by Libranet 3 and Kmail
>~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~


My standard device of choice is a Cisco 2511 (16 ports) or 2509 (8 
ports) with the appropriate octopus cable and connectors.  Very, very 
simple to set up and has "reasonable" security and features.  It's 
big problem is the lack of scrollback buffers, but typically I don't 
care about scrollback buffers and just need to reboot the machine or 
tickle the BIOS.  I only get machines that have serial BIOS control 
(i.e.: SuperMicro.)  There are certainly many other solutions (see 
the advertisements in SysAdmin magazine for a slew of them) but the 
Cisco stuff is pretty easy to get one's hands on and simple to set 
up.  See Google.

I use it just as much for non-PC applications, such as to other 
network gear or components which have serial port craft/console port 
access.

Price: ~$250 on eBay.

JT


From aaron at bavariati.org  Fri Sep 23 15:15:17 2005
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Fri, 23 Sep 2005 15:15:17 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] serial console administration
In-Reply-To: <200509231336.21362.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200509231336.21362.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <20050923221517.GU1773@bavariati.org>

On Fri, Sep 23, 2005 at 01:36:21PM -0700, Carla Schroder wrote:
> Is anyone using the serial console for more than the occasional
> fix-of-last-resort?

Hey, don't disparage those fixes-of-last-resort.  When the pager wakes
you at 3AM, would you rather go clickity-click at your home computer
and go back to bed, or get dressed and drive to the data center?

> Since servers are presumably networked via Ethernet, why would you
> routine server administration over a poky ole serial line?

"Me too" to what Eli and Paul have said.  When I worked there, serial
console was a _must_ at OSDL, both for the production and for the
systems we made available to folks.  You'd be surprised how much nicer
it is to be able to remotely* work on a system without worrying about
its network and sshd falling out from under you.

Another quite valuable thing was that you could capture kernel-panic
and other console messages, and perform Magic SysRQ spells.  The
kernel-hackers appreciated that.

If you have several serial-console systems, Conserver is quite
valuable.  It logs console output and suchlike.

Oh, and remote power-control is the natural complement to
remote-console.  APC makes some nice IP-enabled PDUs.


*For OSDL's purposes, "remotely" means "from anywhere in the world you
can get an Internet connection".


From eli.stair at gmail.com  Fri Sep 23 15:28:26 2005
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Fri, 23 Sep 2005 15:28:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] serial console administration
In-Reply-To: <20050923221517.GU1773@bavariati.org>
References: <200509231336.21362.carla@bratgrrl.com>
	<20050923221517.GU1773@bavariati.org>
Message-ID: <f4468cae05092315281b2896c8@mail.gmail.com>

One note on the specifics this reminded me of:

The Cyclades units I've been using (ACS series) have quite large
configurable console buffers by default (several hundred K). This is
really handy when some system goes down, isn't addressable on the
network and you can console in and get your kernel panic... when your
issue is being caused by either a kernel bug or hardware issue that
doesn't give you the luck of remote or pretty output, capturing that
console dump/bluesmoke output is well worth it... cutnpasting it is
even better :) They also let you configure an NFS mount point (or I
believe syslog) to write console to as well.  Their PDU's integrate
directly with the ACS servers and are controlled by hotkeys from the
same interface.

Not to paint a super-pretty picture, they're costly as hell and
dealing with the internals is a bit odd.  Think of it this way tho, a
few grand total added on as infrastructure cost spread among 32-48
systems isn't bad at all.  It'll save you that few grand several times
over in manpower/travel/downtime production/prozac costs.

/eli

On Fri, 23 Sep 2005, Eli Stair wrote:

> The Cyclades units I've been using (ACS series) [....]

The Cyclades units are the unix systems of serial consoles: you can do 
just about anything with them. I was completely blown away at all the 
configuration options on those things.

The Digi CM units aren't nearly as flexible, though they certainly 
handle standard console setups just fine. I ended up ordering the Digi 
because I've had good prior experience with the company's products, 
it's got Linux under the hood, and it was ca. $1000 cheaper than the 
Cyclades. (I got it on sale from Dell for a few hundred dollars below 
retail price.)

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

I worked for a company that did just that, designing an in-house
networked serial concentrator to avoid having to purchase equipment to
manage its clusters.  When you get right down to it, unless you
literally have nothing better to do with your time (which probably
isn't the case, or you wouldn't mind driving in at 3am :), it's much
easier just to pay for a finished product, even if it's the cheapest
one on the market.

From ehem at m5p.com  Fri Sep 23 16:44:38 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Fri, 23 Sep 2005 16:44:38 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] serial console administration

>From: Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>
> 1. I can replumb network connections -- or kill and completely restart
>     sshd -- remotely.

I guess I might be the odd one out, but sshd is *easy* to deal with while
merely having the ethernet connection. As long as you only kill the
listening process, your session continues to exist and as long as you
verify you've restarted it you're pretty safe. This is what the init
scripts will do, logging users out isn't the job of sshd or its scripts.

At the same time, very useful for doing things with ifconfig.

On 9/23/05, Carla Schroder carla-at-bratgrrl.com wrote:
> Is anyone using the serial console for more than the occasional
> fix-of-last-resort?

Nope.  In fact on my Linux hosts I don't even use it for that since my
experience has been if the linux host is so screwed that you need the
serial port, the serial port isn't working either.  Instead I rely on
dedicated remote-access hardware such as the Dell Remote Access
Controller or HP/Compaq Integrated-Lights-Out card.  These have proven
to be a reliable way to get into a server that's having issues once
they've been set up properly.

If that hardware is too expensive, then perhaps the server doesn't
need that level of availability.

> Since servers are presumably networked via Ethernet, why
> would you routine server administration over a poky ole serial line?

You wouldn't unless you were psychotic.

> Plus I eavesdropped shamelessly on a conversation about updating a Fedora box
> via the serial console, and it took an entire weekend. Yet the person who did
> it thought it was the greatest thing since twist-tops. Soooo... again, if
> Ethernet is working, why would do you this?

See previous comment.  ;-)

  -- Steve

I've even had to upgrade SSL and SSH without rebooting... remotely...

the init'd one and do your dirty-work.

sercon either as long as you have another option.  No more than I'd
use a 300-baud modem just cuz I had it around...

Another use for a serial console is when you are debugging hardware or
perhaps testing a driver.  Frequent resets and power cycles make a
serial connection much more attractive than the network.

In fact I've written and tested a script ment to redo an sshd
installation, while connected via ssh. As long as you keep a spare live
ssh session, it is pretty hard to lose it.

From seniorr at aracnet.com  Fri Sep 23 23:09:37 2005
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 23 Sep 2005 23:09:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] serial console administration
In-Reply-To: <1127539009.14564.66.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <200509231336.21362.carla@bratgrrl.com>
	<1127539009.14564.66.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <86zmq3gfoe.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Vram" == Vram  <lamsokvr at xprt.net> writes:

Vram> Ok..

Vram> I am going to ask the dumb question..

Vram> How do I make this serial console connection?

Usually (in my experience) with a null-modem cable from another
machine.  The null-modem cable (minimally) swaps transmit and receive
lines so that one machine's transmit connects to the other machines
receive and vice versa.  Often, and perferably, the null-modem cable
provides lines to do hardware flow control as well.  You can run
minicom on the serial port of the "supervisor" machine.  The
"supervised" machine runs a getty (in /etc/inittab) on its serial port
to provide a login prompt.

There is also some magic you can do with grub, to let you select
kernels to boot, etc.  The kernel needs to have compiled support for
serial console to see the boot up messages live.

I am sure there is a HOWTO (or equivalent) online somewhere.


-- 
Russell Senior         ``I have nine fingers; you have ten.''

Sarge at the moment, mostly because the hard drive is too small for Gentoo.

I think you can actually buy Thinkpads and some Dell laptops with Linux 
on them from the factory!

From aschlemm at comcast.net  Sun Sep 25 17:45:32 2005
From: aschlemm at comcast.net (Tony Schlemmer)
Date: Sun, 25 Sep 2005 17:45:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linux on Laptops
In-Reply-To: <4336E967.2030704@comcast.net>
References: <4336E967.2030704@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <200509251745.32799.aschlemm@comcast.net>

On Sunday 25 September 2005 11:16, Robert McIntosh wrote:
> I'll be in the market for a laptop in the next month or so and will want
> to run Linux on it (dual boot with Win XP Pro).
>
> I've heard that IBM Thinkpads are a straightforward install with Linux,
> whereas Dell's laptops are nice but difficult to set-up drivers for Linux.
>
> Distro of choice will be Mepis and I'd like to hear some stories of
> Linux on some of the more modern laptops.
>
> Feel free to e-mail directly.
>
> Thanks!
> Robert

I've been running a Dell Inspiron 6000 I got from Costco and it's worked 
pretty well with SuSE 9.2 on it. I plan on upgrading it to SuSE 9.3 as time 
permits. It came with 512MB of memory and an 80GB hard drive, modem, Intel 
Centrino WIFI, and Broadcom wired ethernet port.

Pros:

Seems that the hardware on like video, sound, network, WIFI are all supported 
by Linux. With the Linux source installed I upgraded to a newer version of 
the ipw2200 driver and firmware from Intel. I used WPA at home to secure my 
WIFI network and use "wpa_supplicant" on my laptop. Allows me to connect to 
AES or TKIP encrypted WIFI networks.

Came factory installed with XP Pro which I still need for some things like 
Visio for work.  The SuSE installer had no problems shrinking down the NTFS 
partition leaving plenty of room for Linux swap and filesystem partitions. I 
also create a FAT32 partition that's several GB's in size so I can share 
files between XP and Linux. SuSE automatically detects and mounts the NTFS 
partition read-only for itself so I can read any files from Windows but to 
get files into Windows I need the FAT partition as an exchange area.

Linux seems to be able to control the CPU cycles on the 1.60GhZ Pentium M on 
this laptop. For non-CPU intensive usage like email and web the CPU runs at 
800MHZ but kicks up to the full 1600MHZ when I compile C++ or Java code or do 
other CPU intensive work.

Battery life with a 9 cell battery is pretty decent and I've had this laptop 
running for 4 1/2 hours straight before. Didn't get a low battery warning but 
that's been the longest usage time I've had on the battery so far. YMMV and I 
was mostly just doing email and web access so the CPU was probably running at 
800MhZ to save power.

Cons:

The Intel 915GM chipset for the video wasn't supported by the version of Xorg 
that SuSE 9.2 shipped with. IIRC SuSE 9.2 shipped with Xorg 6.8.1 and I 
needed 6.8.2 to get X working with something other than the Framebuffer 
support. Once working I've got X at the full 1280X800 resultion with 24 bit 
color depth. SuSE had a set of RPMs for X.org 6.8.2 for SuSE 9.2 so it was 
easy getting X upgraded.

The onboard video is intergrated into the mobo and is limited to 8MB of 
memory. Still for office application, email, web and 2D graphics it's more 
than adequate. I saw the laptop also had better graphics available but it was 
using ATI and I wasn't sure if ATI on a laptop would have good drivers or 
not. I did have a work issued Dell inspiron that had an nVIDIA 5200 and it 
was great with the accelerated driver from nVIDIA but I have no experience 
with ATI. Rather than risk potential video problems I went with the safe 
Intel bet as I knew the X.org X server had driver support for the Intel 915GM 
ship even though SuSE 9.2 didn't ship with that suppport initially.

Resolution is limited to 1280x800 which again may be too limited for some 
users.

The monitor is 15.4" wide and so this laptop may be considered too large for 
many users. I carried it around when I was traveling alot to Chicago and have 
a backpack with a padded compartment. It's much easier to carry with the 
weight on both sholders rather than using a briefcase type of laptop case 
where I'd have the weght on one shoulder. The Costco package also included a 
laptop case but it's a briefcase type which I don't care for.

It's a Dell which may or may not be a problem for many people. I know I had 
second thoughts as my company issued Inspiron with a 3GHZ P4 had no less than 
3 mobo replacements. I attribute the failures to the huge amount of heat that 
P4 and 7200 RPM internal hard drive produced. With the Pentium M in this 
Inspiron 6000 the air blowing out of the system from the fan is never hot. 
I've got a 3 year warranty on this laptop that was part of purchase so at 
least I'm covered for the next few years on getting the unit fixed. 

Sorry my post is so long I didn't plan to be so verbose when I started. :-D

Tony

-- 
Anthony Schlemmer
aschlemm at comcast.net

still make sense to buy rather than build.

It's fairly heavy (remember, widescreen, full keyboard including ACTUAL 
number pad, etc.  Holds 2 HDD and a DVD burner or 3 HDD.  You're back up 
significantly with weight.

HOWEVER - I have had 2 problems with this notebook.  The DVD burner (I 
burn a lot of DVDs) went out.  $459 repair.  And currently am having an 
issue with the video card.  $1800 repair.

BUT *********************************
I got the 5 year, next business day onsite service, including accidental 
damage protection.  They are FedExing me a new chassis (everything but 
the LCD) and it costs me $0.  Same with the DVD burner replacement 
(modular slide-in issue).  $0.

The warranty was an extra $500 (normally $900) for that 5-year span - 
will have paid for itself 4 times over and I've only had it a year.  
Remember - accidental damage protection!

A friend of mine 2 days ago just dropped their $2000 Dell notebook and 
smashed it.  The zipper in their briefcase was undone, and it hit the 
concrete.  No warranty - out one brand new notebook.

This computer has a 3 gigahertz DESKTOP P4 with hyperthreading.  Unlike 
95% of the notebooks out there, the processor is SOCKETED instead of 
soldered in.  So if it dies (or I want to upgrade) - then guess what?  
NO ISSUES.

Mine ran about $3000 (I have 2 gigs of RAM, 2 80 gig HDD) with the 
warranty, and I have never been happier.  FOUR USB 2.0 ports (Dell's 
comes with one), Firewire, full numeric keypad, and did I mention the 
widescreen?  I used to have 2 LCD monitors (18" and 19") on my desktop 
machine - this is all I need now.  I can fit my entire office into my 
briefcase - computer, printer (Canon i80) and scanner (Canon i35).  The 
wider softside leather notebook briefcase ran about $75 at Best Buy. 
(Targus)

It is SO worth it to get a full notebook system with warranty.  At 3 
gigahertz, I can run ANYTHING I need, including graphics software under 
an emulator, or virtual machine - absolutely anything.  Do your research 
and find out what features you want.  I wanted:
widescreen
full keyboard
numeric keypad
gigabit ethernet
b/g wireless
4 USB ports for:
    full-size mouse
    printer
    scanner
    thumb drive
DVD burner (rewriteable even)
WARRANTY
firewire
BRIGHT, SHARP screen (best on the market is Gateway's)

There was only one choice for me.  YMMV, but I have never been sorry I 
got this machine, and I now don't own any desktop machines.

RP
