I'm fairly hard on my equipment, traveling a lot.  I also have 2 power 
supplies - one for the office, one for the living room.  Makes life easier.

I have also had Dell and Toshiba equipment - they fail just like 
anything else.  The Gateway (yes, heavier) IS portable, but it DOES 
more, too.  I don't know many notebooks that will allow you 3 drives at 
once.  I handle a lot of data.

The warranty IS worth it.  EVEN IF IT IS MY FAULT I am covered.  That 
means dropping it, spilling liquid, forgetting it's on the roof of one's 
vehicle (happened to several friends), etc.  FOR FIVE YEARS.

Dell's & Toshiba's warranties aren't as good.  If you're getting a $500 
notebook it might not be worth the extra money to some.  Mine ran $3000 
by the time I was through, but 3 gigahertz will STILL be fast in 5 
years.  Notice that clock speeds no longer double every 18 months like 
they used to.  I doubt we'll have 24 or 48 gigahertz machines in 5 
years.  Lucky if we have 10 or 15 gig machines by then, seriously.  This 
machine will still be quite serviceable, and will actually take the 
fastest processor on the market today (remember it's a desktop socket), 
so choosing the platform can be very wise.

A friend of mine works for Dell tech support, supporting warranty 
issues.  His estimation is that notebook drives last right about 12 
months on average.  Should pay for my warranty about once a year then, 
by that alone.  Seeing as I burn DVDs extensively, that will be quite 
worth it alone.

If you are uber-careful and don't ever break anything, fine.  Most 
people aren't.  I spend at least 8 hours a day on the computer, 
sometimes more.  I need something more durable.

LOW-end Gateway computers (and a lot of the older desktops) might not be 
so quality.  The 675XL is the finest notebook I've ever used, and I've 
used Apple, Gateway, Dell, Toshiba (close second IMO), IBM (rugged, but 
hard to work on), Panasonic (VERY durable but they break just the same), 
etc.

From rich at paranoid.org  Mon Sep 26 08:34:00 2005
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Mon, 26 Sep 2005 08:34:00 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] OpenBSD on Portables?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509241121450.28841@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509241121450.28841@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <433814E8.8040505@paranoid.org>

Can I ask the rather sidetracky question of, "Why?" Is this just to 
learn about OpenBSD? Is this laptop holding exteremely sensitive 
information? Or is there something else I'm missing?

OpenBSD is just not the first *nix that comes to my mind when I think 
about workstations even, let alone laptops. If your thought is that 
OpenBSD will be more "lightweight," that may be true, but any Linux/Unix 
can be installed in a more lightweight fashion.

OpenBSD is great, but there is a pretty small development team (at least 
there was the last tinme I checked), and I think you are more likely to 
run into problems with things like drivers. Maybe everything will be 
covered for an older laptop, but if their site does not list it as 
compatible then I wouldn't assume it's going to work.

If I were installing a laptop, I would go with a Linux distro (Ubuntu 
seems to have a pretty small profile) or even FreeBSD if you are a *BSD 
fan. YMMV of course.

As for services, it depends on exactly what you want to do. It's not 
clear to me from your description, but you might not need any.


Rich

From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Sep 26 11:42:44 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 26 Sep 2005 11:42:44 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] serial console administration
In-Reply-To: <1127539009.14564.66.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <200509231336.21362.carla@bratgrrl.com>
	<1127539009.14564.66.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.63.0509261131100.640@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Fri, 23 Sep 2005, Vram wrote:

> I am going to ask the dumb question..
>
> How do  I make this serial console connection?

On the server with the console redirected to the serial port:

1. List ttyS0 in /etc/securetty

2. Start up a getty on ttyS0 in /etc/inittab. Here's an invocation
    that works for Red Hat/Fedora Core/CentOS:

# serial console
con:2345:respawn:/sbin/agetty -h ttyS0 9600 vt100

3. Modify your grub configuration. You'll want to get rid of all
    splashimage and color references. Here's a CentOS version:

serial --unit=0 --speed=9600 --word=8 --parity=no --stop=1
terminal --timeout=5 serial console
default=0
timeout=5
title CentOS-4 i386 (2.6.9-11.ELsmp)
   root (hd0,0)
   kernel /vmlinuz-2.6.9-11.ELsmp ro root=LABEL=/1 console=tty0 console=ttyS0
   initrd /initrd-2.6.9-11.ELsmp.img


Connect another machine to that server using a null-modem cable. Fire 
up minicom (or, better, conserver[1]) on the non-server. Reboot the 
server and enjoy life without a locally attached monitor!

A far more complete explanation can be found in TLDP's Remote Serial 
Console HOWTO[2].

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

[1] RPM spec available online:
     http://www.madboa.com/geek/specs/conserver.spec

[2] http://www.tldp.org/HOWTO/Remote-Serial-Console-HOWTO/

>>>>> "Paul" == Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com> writes:

Paul> Connect another machine to that server using a null-modem
Paul> cable. Fire up minicom (or, better, conserver[1]) on the
Paul> non-server. Reboot the server and enjoy life without a locally
Paul> attached monitor!

On the supervisor machine, I've also set up minicom to run on one of
the virtual consoles (e.g. tty6) like so (from /etc/inittab):

  [...]
  1:2345:respawn:/sbin/getty 38400 tty1
  2:23:respawn:/sbin/getty 38400 tty2
  3:23:respawn:/sbin/getty 38400 tty3
  4:23:respawn:/sbin/getty 38400 tty4
  5:23:respawn:/sbin/getty 38400 tty5
  #6:23:respawn:/sbin/getty 38400 tty6
  6:23:respawn:/usr/bin/openvt -c 6 -w -- /usr/bin/minicom -o -c on serial-console
  [...]

Kind of handy, at least if you've only got a few machines to wrangle.

From jmknoble+plug at pobox.com  Mon Sep 26 13:55:13 2005
From: jmknoble+plug at pobox.com (Jim Knoble)
Date: Mon, 26 Sep 2005 16:55:13 -0400
Subject: [PLUG] OpenBSD on Portables?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509241121450.28841@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509241121450.28841@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20050926205513.GE1950@crawfish.ais.com>

Circa 2005-09-24 14:34 dixit Rich Shepard:

: As my old Toshiba Portege 3025CT has no resale value, I'd like to
: replace the Slackware it's now running with OpenBSD and use it for a
: lightweight communicator and writing machine when I'm away from the
: office.
: 
: The official OpenBSD on Laptops web site does not list this model. I
: find other Toshibas (including the Libretto L70 and a Portege 2000) so
: the OS should install and run on this model.

Probably.  The biggest source of potential problems is liable to be the
PCMCIA bridge.  I once had to build a custom OpenBSD kernel with
different IRQ/ioport settings for the PCMCIA bridge before it would
install properly on a Sony VAIO PCG-505TR.  You may have difficulty
getting sound to work.

: However, I have not found anything -- yet -- on the web or in my
: OpenBSD book that suggests what should be installed on a portable
: rather than on a workstation or server. And, what services should be
: configured for a box that would be used both on the office network and
: stand-alone.

As far as OpenBSD is concerned, there isn't really much difference
between a workstation and a portable; the biggest difference is that
you'll want to turn on apmd to handle power events (but note that there
is no hibernation support, even in current OpenBSD, only support for
suspend).  Make sure you install the x11 bits to begin with and turn on
the AGP aperture at install time---it's easiest that way.

Does your machine have a builtin ethernet port, or will you be using a
PCMCIA ethernet adapter?  OpenBSD doesn't really have an infrastructure
for dealing with running external commands when devices are
hot-plugged---you'll need to run /etc/netstart when you plug in the
ethernet cable (it'll be easiest to configure it with DHCP).

For services, you might consider installing a trusted DNS cache (such as
the one from djbdns <http://cr.yp.to/djbdns.hmtl>) if you're planning on
connecting to untrusted networks.  Otherwise, make believe it's a
workstation, and turn on, say, sshd.  If you want to change profiles
between office and standalone (e.g., say you want to mount an NFS share
while connected to your office network), then you'll want to create a
script or two to manually start or stop the things you'll need (portmap,
for example).  If all you want to run is sshd, i wouldn't worry about
changing profiles.

Also, note that OpenBSD has support for running, say, lpd and similar
services as local-only.  For example, lpd can be told to listen only on
a Unix-domain socket, and no TCP sockets at all (using the -s flag).

A better idea of the things you'd like to do with the notebook would
help me give you better advice.

: Pointers to suggestions are welcome. This is a low-priority project
: for me so if it takes a few months that's perfectly OK.

I've run OpenBSD as both servers and workstations, and i've found it far
easier to get running the way i want it than most Linuxes.  I don't
think it'll take that long.

Good luck,
jim

-- 
jim knoble  |  jmknoble+plug at pobox.com
(GnuPG fingerprint: 809F:09B9:9686:D035:4AB0::9455:124B:0A62:DD6A:76D6)

From bill at bearprinting.com  Mon Sep 26 18:09:35 2005
From: bill at bearprinting.com (Bill Ensley)
Date: Mon, 26 Sep 2005 18:09:35 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Javascript question.
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.58.0509261807380.28980@PPC.DavidMandel.com>

My apologies, I may have missasked my question.

Why does the form field need to be named this way:
	layer_status[1999]

The reason that it's bugging out is that in javascript 

layer_status[1999] is automatically trying to retrieve the

Index 1999 from the layer_status array.

Are you trying to dynamically create a form element?

If so, just use the innerHTML object to add it to a hidden div
And use it as a normal form element.

Ie:
<script>
	function createDynamicField(){
		var div = document.getElementById("hiddenFields");
		div.innerHTML = "<input type='text' name='layer_status_1999'
value=''>";
	}
</script>

<div id="hiddenFields" style="visibility:hidden;">

</div>

If that's not what you are doing, ignore the above.

I have extensive JS knowledge, I'll be online most of the evening, so email
Me back exactly why the field name is so important and we can work it out.

-Bill Ensley
Bear Printing

From plug at lists.discorporate.us  Mon Sep 26 18:13:17 2005
From: plug at lists.discorporate.us (Jason Kirtland)
Date: Mon, 26 Sep 2005 18:13:17 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Javascript question.
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.58.0509261636140.28643@PPC.DavidMandel.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.58.0509261636140.28643@PPC.DavidMandel.com>
Message-ID: <8FE0E78D878791988B88B804@[10.23.5.4]>

David wrote:
>
> Does anyone know javascript?
> [...]
> In my environment I must use the name:
>                layer_status%5B1999%5D or layer_status[1999]
> [... however ...]
>                document.main.layer_status%5B1999%5D.value="PRECIP0";
> [...]
> BLOWS UP.

Javascript has an alternate object indexing syntax for this sort of 
situation:

  object['property'] = 'value';
  object.property = 'value'; // also valid

  document.main['layer_status[1999]'].value = 'foo';
  something['property with spaces'] = 'baz';

Also useful for variable indexing:

  next = array[++someIndex];
  p = 'quux'; return obj[p];

Best,
Jason

> can ping the 0.3 address, but when I try to ping the gateway, I get  
> Destination Host Unreachable and ping: sendmsg: Operation not  
> permitted, with 1 packet transmitted, 0 received.

I don't know if this will help, but...

When my SuSE 9.1 machine goes down due to power failure, I have to use 
the configuration tool (YaST) to reconfigure the Ethernet interface. The 
first time it happened, I discovered that I hadn't told it to use my 
router as the default gateway. The second time, it knew about the 
default gateway, but I still got the "Network Unreachable" error 
message. When I made it think I had made a change (I changed something, 
but then changed it back, so it thought I had edited the configuration), 
it went through the configuration process and all was well.

One of these days, I'll try to find some time to pursue this. Meanwhile, 
I have the work around.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

Note Cards & Photographic Prints available at:
http://home.comcast.net/~rcsphoto/

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Tue Sep 27 22:40:49 2005
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Tue, 27 Sep 2005 22:40:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Moving from FC3 to FC4
In-Reply-To: <1127885222.6038.11.camel@atlas.cadtocam.com>
References: <1127885222.6038.11.camel@atlas.cadtocam.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840509272240774532c3@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/27/05, Kenneth G. Stephens <kens at cad2cam.com> wrote:
> I am getting sleepy and just wondering about upgrading from Fedora FC3
> to FC4.  I should be able to do this using yum, shouldn't I?  I would
> like to change the /etc/fedora-release file to read the FC4 way and then
> do a yum -y update.
>
> This sounds like it **should** work, but must be dangerous.  I could not
> upgrade so easily.

Actually, no, everything I've ever read recommends VERY strongly that
you do an FC3->FC4 upgrade via the installation disks, even if it is a
pain.  You may wish to check out one of the re-spun installation disk
sets to avoid having to start over at FC4 and download all the updates
since then.

http://fedora.isphuset.no/

Work seems to be continuing to integrate yum further with anaconda, so
perhaps FC5 or FC6 will be able to install updates directly...

Jason

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Sep 28 08:28:58 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 28 Sep 2005 08:28:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Wifi Help
In-Reply-To: <61FBF634-91D4-493D-93B0-6042F5A8D090@braithwaites.net>
References: <61FBF634-91D4-493D-93B0-6042F5A8D090@braithwaites.net>
Message-ID: <20050928152858.GB25282@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Tue, Sep 27, 2005 at 02:14:29PM -0700, Ron Braithwaite wrote:
> I'm trying to help a friend with a Motion Computing M1300 Tablet PC,  
> loaded with Mandriva 2005 LE and the 2.6.11 kernel. It has a 1 GHz  
> Centrino that shows up under WinXP (it's a dual boot system) with an  
> Intel PRO/Wireless LAN 2100 3B Mini PCI  wireless connection. It  
> works just fine under WinXP.
> 
> When I do an ifconfig eth2, I see that it has given me 192.168.0.3. I  
> can ping the 0.3 address, but when I try to ping the gateway, I get  
> Destination Host Unreachable and ping: sendmsg: Operation not  
> permitted, with 1 packet transmitted, 0 received.
> 
> This *should* be working, but its not and I don't have a clue as to why.

As root, look at the output of "route" to make sure that you are routing
packets correctly (that is, through eth2).  Also, check that your firewall
permits the packets (it might pass dhcp packets but not ping).  You may
have to turn off eth1 or eth0 (whatever the normal ethernet connection is).  

Also, try pinging the laptop from the gateway, with tcpdump running on
both ends.

Hope that helps;

Keith
(back from England and 8 hours jetlagged)

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From aaronb at personaltelco.net  Wed Sep 28 16:33:34 2005
From: aaronb at personaltelco.net (Aaron Baer)
Date: Wed, 28 Sep 2005 16:33:34 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] WiFi Question
In-Reply-To: <200509281540.31795.chgenly@verizon.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509100958580.9952@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<200509101229.47181.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0509101035550.9952@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<200509281540.31795.chgenly@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <1127950414.8018.12.camel@localhost.localdomain>

On Wed, 2005-09-28 at 15:40 -0700, Chris Genly (verizon) wrote:


> I have an IBM think pad T30 with builtin wireless.  Using the following 
> modules on linux 2.6
> 

Can you figure out exactly what card you have? Groking through lspci
should list what the actually mini-pci card is that you have installed.
If it's the Intel 2200BG, which it seems from their website is what
comes installed, you're not going to want to use the orinoco drivers.
Rather you should be using the drivers that support that card
specifically. 

For the 2200 it's the ipw2200 driver with does support scanning. (It
actually seems to be a really great driver, kismet support and great
sensitivity from the card)

http://ipw2200.sourceforge.net/

It's packaged with the Breezy Kernels.. 

> Is there a way I can get scan working?  
> 

Make sure you're using the correct driver for the card you have. I think
that's your first hurdle. 

A-

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Sep 28 21:43:27 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 28 Sep 2005 21:43:27 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] WiFi Question (T30)
In-Reply-To: <1127950414.8018.12.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509100958580.9952@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<200509101229.47181.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0509101035550.9952@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<200509281540.31795.chgenly@verizon.net>
	<1127950414.8018.12.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <20050929044327.GC28177@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Wed, 2005-09-28 at 15:40 -0700, Chris Genly (verizon) wrote:
> I have an IBM think pad T30 with builtin wireless.  Using the following 
> modules on linux 2.6


On Wed, Sep 28, 2005 at 04:33:34PM -0700, Aaron Baer wrote:
> Can you figure out exactly what card you have? Groking through lspci
> should list what the actually mini-pci card is that you have installed.
> If it's the Intel 2200BG, which it seems from their website is what
> comes installed, you're not going to want to use the orinoco drivers.

The T30 uses a few different kinds of card, depending on the model.
My wife's T30 came with a Cisco mini-PCI card.  A very low power
card, like 5mW, and funky drivers.   And it was hard to power
control for plane flights, necessitating use of the Cisco control
panel.  Difficult to switch between our "inner" wifi network (WEPed)
and a public network.  All those things suggested that built-in
wireless - especially Cisco - was not the way to go. 

So we yanked the mini-PCI card and use a $40 200mW Senao/Prism2 card,
which is guaranteed to go off the air when you pull the card, and is
easier to configure for WEP or open connection.  This may seem like a
step backward compared to built-in, but it is actually easier to use.
It worked for three weeks in Sweden and England, at two open/public
sites and two WEPed hotel sites.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

The ipw2x00 drivers are F/OSS but still need binary firmware, which
are not supplied with Fedora Core 4 (or Extras) but _is_ in the Livna
repository. We ran in to this at the last clinic. As you said, it's
bundled w/ the Ubuntu Breezy kernels. You can also just D/L it from
the aforementioned SF page after clicking through their license.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Thu Sep 29 09:03:56 2005
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)

Subject: [PLUG] Network Connection Tools
Message-ID: <433C106C.3040000@comcast.net>

Is there a tool I can use at my end to try to pinpoint a communication 
problem between me and a specific web site?

This morning I was taking an on-line quiz at a site I've been using for 
several months. Over the months, there has always been a variable amount 
of lag between hitting "next" and getting the next question. This 
morning, that lag was substantially worse, although still not consistent 
-- sometimes I'd get the next question almost instantly, other times I 
would wait for 20-30 seconds. Finally, after a particularly long wait, I 
got a time-out error (sorry -- I didn't write it down), and the quiz reset.

I called the provider of the quiz who checked his site logs and saw now 
apparent problems at his end. Our assumption is that the problem is a 
communications delay somewhere in the middle, perhaps with Comcast. 
Thus, the question about how to find that problem.

> Is there a tool I can use at my end to try to pinpoint a communication

I like 'mtr' for figuring out where the delays are.

I've seen a 'visual traceroute' for Windog that gives very interesting
views of your routes, but there doesn't seem to be anything like that
for Linux.

Subject: [PLUG] Regex question
In-Reply-To: <20050930160041.55176.qmail@web52610.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20050930160041.55176.qmail@web52610.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20050930161335.GA1199@dalsemi.com>

On Fri, Sep 30, 2005 at 09:00:41AM -0700, Scott Van Hoosen wrote:
> I'm trying to use perl and a regular expression to find all instances
> of "Fred" in a file except for "Fred Flintstone" and "Fred Mertz".
> Here's the closest I found, but doesn't quite work:
> 
>   /Fred [^(Mertz|Flintstone)]/
> 
> It only compares the first letters of Mertz and Flintstone, so it also
> filters out "Fred Magoo" and "Fred Meyer". How can I negate a string
> rather than a single character?

There are lots of ways to do it.  You could use two regular expressions
and logic in between them.  You could read the perlre manpage and learn
about negative lookaheads.

But using [] builds a character class, and everything inside of it
is used on a character by character basis.  So it will reject Fred
followed by a space and then none of )(|MertzFlinso.

Good luck on your homework,
Colin


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Fri Sep 30 09:19:51 2005
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Fri, 30 Sep 2005 09:19:51 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Regex question
In-Reply-To: <20050930160041.55176.qmail@web52610.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20050930160041.55176.qmail@web52610.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <200509300919.51883.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Scott Van Hoosen
# on Friday 30 September 2005 09:00 am:

> /Fred [^(Mertz|Flintstone)]/

read 'perldoc perlre'

You want a negative assertion instead of a character class.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Sep 30 09:46:55 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)

Subject: [PLUG] Regex question
In-Reply-To: <20050930160041.55176.qmail@web52610.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20050930160041.55176.qmail@web52610.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.63.0509300941180.4079@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Fri, 30 Sep 2005, Scott Van Hoosen wrote:

> I'm trying to use perl and a regular expression to find all 
> instances of "Fred" in a file except for "Fred Flintstone" and "Fred 
> Mertz". Here's the closest I found, but doesn't quite work:
>
>  /Fred [^(Mertz|Flintstone)]/

/Fred\s(?!(Mertz|Flintstone))/

In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0509270808300.14148@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Tuesday 27 September 2005 08:12, Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Mon, 26 Sep 2005, Jim Knoble wrote:
> > Probably.  The biggest source of potential problems is liable to be the
> > PCMCIA bridge. I once had to build a custom OpenBSD kernel with different
> > IRQ/ioport settings for the PCMCIA bridge before it would install
> > properly on a Sony VAIO PCG-505TR. You may have difficulty getting sound
> > to work.
>
>    Thanks, Jim. Actually, rationality returned the next day and I
> acknowledged that it makes much more sense to update the Slackware
> installation on this notebook than futz with a new OS just because I have
> the book and intended to build a firewall with OpenBSD. I'm using the
> Netgear FVS318 firewall appliance and I don't need an uber-secure OS on the
> lightweight notebook.
>
>    Besides, I have too many higher-priority needs and none requires a
> specific OS.
>
> Rich

The FVS318 is a pretty decent firewall and I say this as someone that has used 
OpenBSD for a number of years now for a firewall at several sites. I've been 
using an FVS318 as a a no fuss replacement for my OpenBSD firewall when I 
need to take the system down for an upgrade of the version of OpenBSD on it. 
I've also worked at a startup and had my non-computer techincal boss put me 
on the stop at CompUSA for a firewall for our office. I picked the FVS318 
since it had the ability to support up to 8 IPSec tunnels which was great 
since we had a few people working offsite.

Due to lack of time I still haven't gotten my firewall deployed with OpenBSD 
3.7 on it so the FVS318 has been running for nearly a month now. :-/  I've 
got my firewall system running, installed all of the core OpenBSD source code  
and I even got all of the source patches applied to my codebase. After that I 
let the system go and recompile everything so I can apply source patches as 
they are released.

OpenBSD is a handful to maintain unless you have some significant time to 
invest getting a system setup so you can apply source code patches and 
recompile the code that was patched. Since I cannot predict what code will be 
patched I compile all of the core code and basically release my own build of 
OpenBSD. It's not too bad with a few shell scripts  to automate the build and 
release process. 

But because of the work involved in setting up an OpenBSD I usually run the 
same OpenBSD version and only upgrade the version of OpenBSD I run once the 
development team no longer releases patches for the version I'm running. I 
have found I can speed up the setup process by keeping my OpenBSD 
configuration files in my own CVS server and so once I have basic network 
connectivity working I just pull my firewall and other configuration files 
out of CVS. 

Tony

Anthony Schlemmer
aschlemm at comcast.net
